[1] The first part
[2] Police must follow the albatross's method of operation - knowing that the hands and clubs of the hobos will bring them great misfortune, they still lay their eggs on the beach... police are no exception. All the process of incubation should not be disturbed by any Japanese.
[3] Extracted from Yu Chuan's "Chien Yeh Collection"
[4] Chapter 1: Theater Audience and Corpses
[5] The opening of the 1920s drama festival was embarrassing. Eugene O'Neill forgot to create a new play in time, so he couldn't get financial support from the "cultured" people. As for the "common people", after watching a few passionless plays, they gave up the formal theater and went to the movie house to find simpler pleasure.
[6] On Monday evening, September 24th, a light drizzle covered the brightly lit Broadway theater district from 37th Street to Columbus Circle. Desolate theater managers and directors could be seen everywhere. Several plays were canceled on the spot by higher-ups, hoping that God and the weather bureau would witness their plight. The rain kept people who should have been at the theater at home by their radios and card tables. Only a few brave souls ventured out into the deserted streets in the rain. Broadway was indeed in a gloomy mood.
[7] However, on the sidewalk in front of the Roman Theater on 47th Street, west of "White Street", there was a crowd of spectators who only came out during the mid-period of the theater festival when the weather was fine. The signboard of "The Gunfight" shone brilliantly under the decorative awning of the theater entrance. The cashier skillfully served the crowd of people waiting in front of the "Today's Performance" window. The doorman wore a sober uniform of yellow and blue, his demeanor refined by the passage of time, and was therefore very conspicuous. He bowed to lead the evening's audience into the front row of the auditorium. These spectators wore high hats and leather coats, with satisfied expressions on their faces, as if nothing could stop them from watching "The Gunfight" performance.
[8] In the latest theater on Broadway, people were hurriedly looking for their seats, obviously a little anxious, as the play's noisy characteristics were well known. At this point, the last chattering audience stopped, the last audience to arrive was tripped by his neighbor, and the lights gradually dimmed, and the curtain slowly rose. A gunshot echoed, a man screamed... the show began.
[9] "The Gunfight" is the first play of the drama festival to use the characteristic sound effects of the mob. Automatic pistols, machine guns, attacks on nightclubs, mob killings that kill enemies with loud noises - all the clichéd ways of romanticizing the mob society were woven into the fast-paced three-act play. It exaggeratedly reflected the era - a little vulgar, a little lowbrow, but all in all, it satisfied the audience who watched the play. Therefore, whether it was sunny or rainy, the play was always full and sold out. The situation in the theater tonight was enough to prove its popularity.
[10] The performance progressed smoothly. The first act reached its climax with an explosion, and the audience shuddered. The rain had stopped, and people used the ten-minute break after the first act to step out of the theater and breathe in the fresh air after the rain. Then, the curtain slowly rose, and the second act began. The explosions on the stage were even louder. With the sharp-tongued dialogue behind the stage lights, the second act quickly entered the crucial moment. The slight commotion at the back of the theater did not attract attention, as it was normal in the noise and darkness. It seemed that no one noticed anything was wrong, and the plot continued to unfold with a bang. However, the noise of the disturbance gradually increased. At this point, several audience members in the back row on the left began to stir in their seats, angrily defending their rights. This protest was highly contagious, and soon dozens of eyes turned to the front row of the auditorium.
[11] Suddenly, a piercing scream echoed through the theater. The audience, engrossed in the rapid events unfolding on stage, craned their necks expectantly in the direction of the scream, hoping for a new dramatic twist in the plot.
[12] The theater lights suddenly snapped on, illuminating bewildered, terrified, and enraptured faces, all satisfied with the plot. Near the leftmost closed exit, a tall policeman stood, grabbing the arm of a slightly tense-looking man. The policeman pushed away a crowd of inquisitive people with a loud voice, shouting, "Stay in your seats and don't move!"
[13] The audience burst into laughter.
[14] But the smiles quickly faded. As the audience began to realize that even the actors looked confused and hesitant, despite continuing to recite their lines behind the stage lights, occasionally casting puzzled glances at the front row of the auditorium. People stood up from their seats, uneasy in the face of the anticipated tragedy. The officer's authoritative voice continued to roar, "I said, sit still! Stay where you are!"
[15] The audience suddenly realized that this was not a play but reality. Women screamed, desperately clutching their companions. The upper-level audience was in a panic, despite not being able to see what was happening downstairs.
[16] The officer turned sharply to a small, dark-skinned man dressed in evening attire, standing nearby and fidgeting with his hands.
[17] "Mr. Pensler, I need you to close every exit immediately and make sure they are all locked," he barked. "Assign a usher to each entrance to prevent anyone from entering or leaving. Have someone guard the alley until the police arrive. Move quickly, Mr. Pensler, or there will be trouble."
[18] Some excited audience members disregarded the officer's loud warnings and crowded around to ask questions. The small, dark-skinned man pushed them away and hurried off.
[19] The officer spread his legs and stood at the last exit on the left, using his massive frame to block a person lying on the floor between two rows of seats. The person was dressed in evening attire and lay in an odd position. The officer looked up, grabbed the trembling man beside him, and quickly scanned the back of the auditorium.
[20] "Hey, Nelson!" he called.
[21] A tall, blond-haired man quickly emerged from the small room next to the main exit, pushing through the crowd and approaching the policeman. He looked sharply at the motionless body on the floor.
[22] "What happened, Dohr?" he asked.
[23] "You'd better ask this guy," the policeman said sternly, shaking the arm he was holding. "Someone died, and this one—" he glared fiercely at the huddled little man, "Bosack, William Bosack. Mr. Bosack here says,” Dohr stammered, "Mr. Bosack says he heard the dead man whisper that he was murdered."
[24] Nelson stared at the body, dumbfounded.
[25] The policeman bit his lip. "This is a real pain in the ass, Harry," he croaked. "I'm the only cop here, and I have to deal with a bunch of hysterical idiots... I need your help."
[26] "Sure, whatever you say—this is quite a mystery."
[27] Doris suddenly turned around and shouted at the man sitting three rows ahead who was staring at him, "Hey, get down here! And you guys, back to your seats! Now! Anyone who meddles gets a punch from me!"
[28] He turned to Nelson. "Harry, get back to your office and call headquarters. Tell them there's a murder here. Have them send a squad over—bring a lot of them. Tell them it's at the theater—they'll know what to do. And, Harry—take my whistle and blow it outside. I need help."
[29] Nelson walked back through the crowd, and Doris called after him, "Harry, make sure they send old Quin over."
[30] The man with light brown hair disappeared from view, returning to his office. Soon, a sharp whistle echoed through the sidewalk in front of the theater.
[31] The dark-skinned theater manager followed Doral's orders to have people guard the exits and alleys, and then he rushed back through the crowd. His evening dress shirt was wrinkled, and he wiped his forehead, looking flustered. As he turned to go forward, a woman stopped him.
[32] She said sternly, "Mr. Pensler, what gives this policeman the right to keep us here? I tell you, I have the right to leave! I don't care what's going on here - it's none of my business - it's your business - tell him not to do this stupid thing of locking up innocent people!"
[33] The little man tried to escape while stammering, "Oh, ma'am, please listen to me. I'm sure this officer knows what he's doing. Someone was killed here - it's serious. You don't see... I'm the theater manager, and I have to follow his orders... please calm down... be patient..."
[34] He slipped away from her before she could object.
[35] Doral waved his arms frantically and shouted from his seat, "Everyone, sit down and be quiet! I don't care if you're the mayor, you - the one with glasses - sit down, or I'll take action! Don't you know what's going on? Be quiet, I said!” He jumped down from the seat, wiping the sweat from the brim of his hat and muttering to himself.
[36] The main floor was filled with agitation and panic, like a boiling pot. People on the upper levels stretched their necks over the railings, trying to figure out the cause of the commotion below, but to no avail. The audience had forgotten that the performance on stage had been interrupted. Actors mumbled lines from the script by the stage footlights, but the words had lost their meaning. The curtain slowly fell, and the evening's performance ended. The actors chattered nervously as they hurried down the steps. They, like the audience, stared in confusion at the scene of the incident.
[37] Among these actors, the plump and radiant elderly lady named Hilda Orlancy, an excellent guest actress, was portrayed as "Barmaid Murphy" on the poster; the delicate and elegant "street urchin Nettie" was played by Eve Ellis, the female lead; James Peir, the sturdy and tall male lead in "The Gunfight," wore a tweed suit and a hat; Stephen Barry, the young man, looked smart in his evening wear, playing the role of an upper-class youth falling into the "gangster" trap. Lucy Hallton's portrayal of the "streetwalker" attracted harsh criticism from drama critics during this inappropriate drama festival, as they had little else to criticize or accuse. The elderly man with a pointed beard wore a flawless evening gown, proving that M. Ler Blond, the costume supplier for "The Gunfight," was a remarkable tailor. As the bulky villain looked down at the chaotic audience, his anger on stage disappeared, and a rare gentleness appeared on his face. In fact, all the theatrical personnel, wearing wigs and makeup, rushed down the stage steps, running slowly under the falling curtain, rushing down to the front row of the main floor, and pushing their way through the aisles towards the scene of the disturbance.
[38] At the main entrance of the theatre, another commotion made people disregard Dahl's order to stay put and stand up to see what was going on. A group of people in blue uniforms pushed their way in, holding batons. Dahl sighed deeply and saluted the tall man in plainclothes who led them.
[39] "What's going on, Dahl?" the newcomer asked, frowning at the chaos around him. The blue-uniformed men pushed the audience back to the seating area in the front row. Standing people tried to sneak back to their seats, but were caught and then pushed to the back row, joining the angry crowd.
[40] "Report, Chief, this person seems to have been murdered," Dahl said.
[41] "Ah, ha," the plain-clothes man glanced indifferently at the motionless body lying at their feet, its face covered by a black-sleeved arm, legs awkwardly spread out, extending under the front row chairs.
[42] "What happened - a shooting?" the newcomer asked Doyle, turning his eyes.
[43] "No, sir - it doesn't seem to be,” the officer replied. "I immediately had a doctor from the audience come and check - he thinks it's poisoning."
[44] The police chief grumbled, "Who is this?" He pointed to Poussac, shivering beside Doyle, and suddenly shouted loudly.
[45] "The one who found the body," Doyle answered. "He hasn't left the scene."
[46] "Good," the detective turned to the crowd of people crammed a few feet behind him and asked, "Who is the manager here?"
[47] Poulson stepped forward.
[48] "I'm Wiley, the chief of detectives from headquarters," the plainclothes man growled. "Didn't you make these idiots quiet?"
[49] "I did my best, Chief," the manager rubbed his hands and mumbled, "but they seem to be angry at the policeman's actions," he pointed apologetically at Doyle, "he shouted at them. I don't know how to get them to sit down as if nothing had happened."
[50] "Oh, we'll handle it," Wiley interrupted him and quickly gave orders to the uniformed men by his side.
[51] "Hey –" he turned to Dorr and called, "how about the doors and exits? Have you taken any measures?"
[52] "Of course, Inspector," the policeman grinned, "I've had Mr. Panzer assign ushers to guard every entrance. They've been there all night. But I've also specifically instructed them to ensure that everything is secure."
[53] "Good. Has anyone tried to leave?"
[54] "I can assure you, Inspector," Panzer interjected, "that we need ushers almost at every exit for this play. It's a crime drama with lots of gunshots, screams, and so on. Having someone at every door enhances the mystery. I can easily check for you if –"
[55] "We'll handle it ourselves." Wheeler said, "Dorr, have you asked anyone to come over?"
[56] "I called Inspector Quinn," Dorr replied, "I had this publicist Nelson call headquarters."
[57] Wiley's cold face cracked a slight smile. "You're quite thorough. And what about this body? Has anyone touched it since this guy found it?"
[58] The man, tightly held by Dorr and huddled up, cried, "I — I just found him, sir — I swear to God, I —"
[59] "Alright, alright," Wiley said coldly, "Are you going to cry all night? What's there to cry about? Hmm, Dorr?"
[60] "No one has touched it since I arrived," Dorr answered, a hint of pride in his voice, "Of course, except for Dr. Stutgart. I had him called from the audience to confirm that the man was dead. No one else has come close."
[61] "You've been busy, Doyle?" Wiley said. He whirled around to face Pensler, who stepped back. "Mr. Manager, you'd better go up on stage and announce that everyone stays put until Officer Quinn says they can leave—understand? Tell them to cooperate, the more they cooperate, the sooner they'll be out of here. And make it clear that they stay in their seats, anyone who causes trouble is a suspect."
[62] "Yes, yes. My God, what a mess!" Pensler grumbled as he made his way down the aisle towards the stage.
[63] Just then, a small group of people pushed through the stage door at the back of the theater and stepped onto the carpet.
[64] Chapter 2: One Quinn Works, Another Quinn Observes
[65] Detective Richard Quinn was an unremarkable man in both form and demeanor. The elderly gentleman was short and gaunt, with a kindly, placid face. He walked with a slight stoop but moved with a measured pace; he had a full head of white hair and a thick beard, a pair of gray eyes that sparkled with intelligence, and long, slender hands that seemed to complement his demeanor perfectly.
[66] As Quine walked quickly across the carpet, eyes from all directions fell upon him, but he left no lasting impression. However, his refined and imposing aura was unique, and his wrinkled face broke into a warm smile, causing a murmur among the audience, which blended perfectly with the rhythm of his progress.
[67] The police's changes were more obvious. Doyle retreated to the corner of the left exit. Wiley, the Chief Inspector, stood calmly next to the corpse - while everyone else was almost hysterical, he remained mocking and indifferent. He seemed to willingly give up his central position. The police guarding the aisle saluted the inspector. The tense, grumbling, and angry audience inexplicably relaxed and sat back heavily.
[68] Inspector Quine walked up to Wiley and shook his hand.
[69] "It's really a pity, Thomas. I heard about it when you were about to go home." He whispered. He smiled at Doyle like a loving father and then glanced at the man on the ground with a hint of pity. "Thomas," he asked, "are all the exits guarded?" Wiley nodded.
[70] The old man turned around and scanned the surroundings of the scene with interest. He asked Wiley a question quietly, who nodded in agreement; then he beckoned Doyle over with his finger.
[71] "Doyle, where are the people sitting in these positions?" He pointed to the three chairs next to the corpse and the four chairs in the front row.
[72] Doyle looked puzzled. "I don't see anyone, Inspector... ..."
[73] Quinn stood silently for a moment, gesturing for Doyle to return, and then whispered to Wiley, "It's a crowded room... Do you remember the case?" Wiley frowned solemnly. "I feel that this case is a bit tricky," the inspector continued in a gentle tone, "What I can see now is a dead man and a group of sweaty people making a commotion. Let Hessey and Piggott direct the crowd, hmm, child?"
[74] Wiley shouted for the two plainclothes officers who had entered the theater with him. The two men turned around, struggling to squeeze their way to the back, pushing people aside. The police joined the two detectives in their action. The actors were ordered to retreat. The police cordoned off the middle row of seats with a rope, and about fifty people squeezed into the narrow space. The police quietly surrounded them, instructing them to show their tickets and then returning to their seats one by one. Within five minutes, all the audience had returned to their seats. The actors were told to stay within the rope for the time being.
[75] At the leftmost aisle, Inspector Quinn reached into his coat pocket and carefully took out a beautifully carved brown snuffbox, enjoying the sensation as he took a pinch of snuff.
[76] "That's about right, Thomas," he chuckled. "You know I hate noise... Who is this poor chap on the ground? Do you know?" Wiley shook his head. "I haven't touched this body, officer. I only arrived a few minutes before you. A man from the 47th Street precinct called me from a phone booth, reporting Doll's whistle. Doll has been doing a good job, keeping an eye on things... His superior praised him in the report."
[77] "Oh," said the officer, "Yes, yes. Doll. Come here, Doll."
[78] Doll approached and saluted.
[79] "What happened here just now, Doll?" asked the short, white-haired old man, comfortably leaning back in his chair.
[80] "All I know is, officer," said Doll, "a few minutes before the second act ended, this man –" he pointed to the pitifully standing Puskas in the corner, "I was standing at the back watching the show, and he came over and said, 'Sir, someone has been murdered! Someone has been murdered!' He was crying like a child, and I thought he was drunk. But I came over – it was dark here, and there were gunshots and shouting on stage – and then I looked at this guy on the ground. I didn't touch him, but I felt his chest and found no heartbeat. To confirm if he was dead, I called a doctor – a Mr. Stuttgart – to help me check... "
[81] Inspector Quin stood up abruptly, tilting his head like a parrot. "Great," he said, "great, Doralee. I'll ask Stuttgart later. What happened next?" he asked.
[82] "Then," the policeman continued, "I sent the usheress down this corridor to find Mr. Poulsen, Louis Poulsen - the manager over there..."
[83] Inspector Quin nodded as he watched Poulsen talking to Nelson a few feet away.
[84] "That's Poulsen you're talking about. Okay, okay... Ellery! Did you get my message?"
[85] He pushed Poulsen aside (Poulsen apologized and stepped back), and approached a tall young man who was entering through the door, taking a leisurely look around. The old man took the young man's arm.
[86] "Didn't bother you too much, son? Which bookstore did you visit tonight? Ellery, I'm so glad you came! I reached into my pocket, took out a snuff box, and inhaled deeply — too deeply, causing a sneeze — and then looked up at my son's face.
[87] "In fact," Ellery Quinn impatiently turned his eyes, "I can't reciprocate your compliments. You dragged me out of a paradise for book lovers. I was just convincing the owner to sell me the first edition of Faulkner's novel, a priceless treasure, and I was planning to borrow money from you at the headquarters. I called you over there — so I came. A Faulkner novel — uh, never mind, I'll buy it tomorrow."
[88] The officer chuckled. "If you tell me you found an antique snuff box, I might be interested. Looks like — come on, we seem to have work to do tonight."
[89] The old man tugged at his son's coat sleeve, walking towards a small group of people on the left. Ellery Quinn was six inches taller than his father, with hair cut neatly along the contours of his face, reaching his shoulders, and swaying rhythmically with his movements. He wore a dark gray coat, holding a walking stick, and a pair of spectacles that seemed out of proportion to his robust physique. However, his furrowed brows, long lines on his face, and piercing eyes suggested that he was more of a thinker than an athlete.
[90] They approached the group of people near the body. Detective Weil greeted Ellery respectfully. Ellery leaned over the back of a chair, glancing at the corpse, and then stepped back.
[91] "Continue, Dole," the officer said cheerfully. "You looked at the body, grabbed the person who found him, called the manager... and then what?"
[92] "Penzel immediately closed all the doors at my command to ensure that no one entered or exited," Dole answered. "Many spectators complained, but nothing else happened."
[93] "Good, good!" the officer said, fumbling for his snuff. "You did well. Now - that gentleman over there."
[94] He gestured to the small man shivering in the corner, who hesitantly approached, licking his lips, looking helplessly at him, and standing silently.
[95] "What's your name?" the officer asked pleasantly.
[96] "Poussac—William Poussac," the man said, "I'm an accountant, sir. I was about to—"
[97] "One thing at a time, Poussac. Where were you sitting?"
[98] Poussac eagerly pointed to the sixth seat next to the aisle in the last row. A young lady sat in the fifth seat, staring at them nervously.
[99] "Understood," the officer said, "Is that young lady with you?"
[100] "Yes, yes... yes, sir. She's my fiancée, sir. Her name is Esther—Esther Jablow..."
[101] A detective near the back was scribbling notes in his notebook. Ellery stood behind his father, scanning one exit after another. He pulled a book from his coat pocket and sketched a diagram on the inside cover.
[102] The officer looked at the girl, who quickly averted her gaze. "Now, Bosack, I'd like to hear what happened."
[103] "I... I didn't do anything illegal, sir."
[104] Inspector Quine patted Bosack's shoulder. "No one said you did, Bosack. I just want to know what happened. Take your time—say it however you want—"
[105] Bosack glanced curiously at him, moistened his lips, and began. "Well, I was sitting there, with my—with Miss Jablow. We really enjoyed the show. The second act was quite thrilling—lots of gunshots and shouting—and then I stood up to walk down the aisle—this aisle—here." He nervously pointed to the carpet where he had been standing. Inspector Quine nodded sympathetically.
[106] "I had to squeeze past my...Miss Jablow. She was only one person away from the aisle, so I went that way. I didn't want to—" He apologetically paused, "interrupt anyone during the most exciting part, so I didn't go the other way..."
[107] "You're quite the gentleman, Pascal." The officer smiled.
[108] "Yes, sir. So I groped my way along the row, as the theater was quite dark. Then I walked...up to this person." He shuddered and spoke more quickly, "I thought his posture was strange. His knees were against the seat in front, and I couldn't squeeze past. I said, 'I'm sorry.' Then I tried again, but his knees still didn't move. I didn't know what to do, sir—I'm not as bold as others, and I was about to turn back when suddenly his body slid to the floor—I was still right next to him. Of course, I was a little frightened—that's only natural..."
[109] "I suppose you were quite startled," the officer said sympathetically. "What happened next?"
[110] "Well, sir...I didn't have time to react before he completely fell out of his seat and hit his head on my leg. I didn't know what to do, and I couldn't call for help—I just couldn't...I just naturally bent down to him, thinking he was drunk or sick or something, to help him up. I didn't think about what to do next."
[111] "I can understand your feelings, Mr. Baskerville. Go on."
[112] "Later, I reported this to the police. As soon as I lifted his head, I felt his hand reach out to grab me, as if he was trying to hold onto something, and he made a groaning sound. It was too low for me to hear very well, but it was terrible. I couldn't make it out..."
[113] "We're making progress now." The policeman said. "What happened next?"
[114] "Then he spoke. Not really spoke - more like a gurgling sound, as if he was choking. He said a few words, but I couldn't make them out. But I realized he wasn't sick or drunk, so I bent down even lower, trying to listen. He said something like 'This is murder...I was murdered...' or something like that..."
[115] "Did he say 'murder'?" The policeman looked intently at Baskerville, his expression serious. "Well, I'm sure it must have been quite a shock to you, Mr. Baskerville. But did you hear him say 'murder' for sure?"
[116] "That's what I heard, sir. I have good hearing."
[117] "Oh!" Quine's expression relaxed, and he smiled again. "Of course. I just wanted to confirm. So, what did you do next?"
[118] "Then I felt him twitch, and suddenly his body went limp in my arms. I was worried he was dead, and I didn't know what to do - but then I went to report it to the police - this police officer." He pointed to the indifferent, swaying Dole.
[119] "Is that all?"
[120] "Yes, sir. Yes, sir. That's all I know." Bessac said, relieved.
[121] Quinn grabbed the front of Baskerville's coat and shouted, "That's not all, Baskerville. You forgot to tell us why you left your seat!"
[122] Baskerville coughed, swayed back and forth, and seemed unsure of what to say next. He leaned in close to the officer and whispered something that surprised him.
[123] "Oh!" Quinn's lips curled into a skeptical smile, but he said seriously, "I see, Baskerville. Thank you for your help. Now, it's all right—you can go back to your seat and leave with the others later." He waved him off. Baskerville glanced palely at the corpse on the ground, walked around the last row of seats, and reappeared next to the girl. They immediately started whispering to each other.
[124] The officer turned to Wiley with a smile, and Ellery made an impatient gesture, about to speak, then reconsidered, and quietly retreated. "Well, Thomas," the officer sighed, "let's take a look at this guy."
[125] He crouched down quickly next to the corpse, kneeling in the gap between the last row and the front row. Although the overhead lights were bright, the narrow space near the floor was dim. Wiley took out a flashlight and squatted next to the officer, the beam of light from the flashlight illuminating the body as the officer's hand moved. Quinn silently pointed to an unattractive irregular brown spot on the dead man's chest. The front of the shirt was otherwise spotless.
[126] "Blood?" Willie murmured.
[127] The officer sniffed the shirt carefully. "Nothing is more dangerous than whiskey," he retorted.
[128] His fingers quickly explored the body, feeling the heart and neck, where the collar was loosened. He looked up at Willie.
[129] "It seems to be poisoned. Well, Thomas, call Dr. Stuttgart over, please? I want to hear the opinion of this expert before Pluto arrives."
[130] Willie immediately gave the order. After a moment, a medium-sized man in evening dress, with olive skin and sparse black beard, followed a detective.
[131] "He's here, officer," Wiley said.
[132] "Oh, well. Quinn stopped examining, lifted his head, "Hello, doctor. I heard you came to check when the body was found. I can't see any obvious cause of death - what's your opinion?"
[133] "My examination was certainly hasty," Dr. Stuttgart said cautiously, brushing his satin collar as if wiping a stain, "The lighting was dim, and at first I couldn't notice any unusual signs of death. From the facial muscles, I thought it was heart failure, but after a closer examination, I noticed his face was turning blue - you can see it clearly under this light, right? Combined with the alcohol smell in his mouth, it seems to be some kind of alcohol poisoning. There's one thing I can guarantee - he didn't die from gunshot or stabbing. I can tell that right away. To rule out strangulation, I even checked his neck - you can see I loosened his collar.
[134] "I understand," the officer smiled. "Thank you very much, doctor. Oh, and," as Dr. Stuttgart mumbled and turned his face, the officer added, "Do you think he could have been methanol poisoned?"
[135] Dr. Stuttgart answered without hesitation, "Impossible. It's a more toxic and faster-acting thing."
[136] "Can you tell me exactly what kind of poison this person died from?"
[137] The olive-skinned doctor hesitated for a moment and then said stiffly: "I'm sorry, officer, you can't expect me to be more precise. In this environment... His voice trailed off, and he gradually retreated.
[138] Quinn smiled softly and bent down to continue his work.
[139] The dead man lay sprawled on the ground, an unseemly sight. The officer gently lifted the clenched hand and stared intently at the distorted face. He then checked under the chair, finding nothing. However, a black silk-lined cape seemed to be carelessly draped over the back of the chair. His hand probed inside the clothes, emptying all the pockets of the suit and cape. He pulled out several letters and documents from the inside pocket of his chest, then delved into the pockets of his vest and pants, dividing the items into two piles - one pile was made up of documents and letters, and the other pile consisted of coins, keys, and various small items. He also found a silver long-necked bottle engraved with the initials "M.F." in the back pocket of his pants. He picked up the bottle carefully, holding the neck, examining the polished surface, seemingly looking for fingerprints. After shaking his head, he wrapped the bottle with a clean handkerchief and placed it aside.
[140] There was a blue ticket stub with the inscription "LL32 Left," which he stealthily tucked into his vest pocket.
[141] He didn't stop checking other items, searching through the lining of the waistcoat and coat, and quickly felt down the trouser legs. When his fingers touched the pocket at the back of the coat, he exclaimed softly, "Ah-ha, Thomas, a major discovery!" He then pulled out a small evening bag for ladies, adorned with shining rhinestones.
[142] He thoughtfully turned the bag over in his hand, then snapped it open, quickly glancing inside, and removed some female accessories. In a small compartment, he found a lipstick, which was snugly next to a business card holder. After a while, he put everything back in its place and stuffed the bag into his pocket. The officer picked up the documents from the ground and quickly scanned through them. When he came to the last one - a sealed letterhead - he frowned.
[143] "Have you heard of Monte Field, Thomas?" he asked, looking up.
[144] Wiley bit his lip. "Of course. One of the city's most unscrupulous lawyers."
[145] The officer spoke solemnly: "Well, Thomas, this is Monte Field - his corpse." Wiley grumbled.
[146] "The failure of the police system lies in the fact that," Erlie's voice came from behind his father, "someone has to deal with a poisonous creature like Monte Field, and is ruthlessly pursued."

[503] "Of course, sir. Who said otherwise?" The girl became agitated. Quinn looked at her trembling fingers, and they stopped moving.
[504] "What do you mean?" she asked.
[505] "I mean, you told me earlier that you were in your usual spot at the beginning of the show; you led many people to their seats; you don't remember if you ever led Monte Field, the deceased, to his seat; during the performance, you stood at the front of the left aisle the whole time. Is that right, Maggie?"
[506] "Shut up, Maggie," Johnny interrupted unexpectedly, "Don't make things worse. Either way, he'll find out we sat together, and he'll suspect you. You don't know this guy. Fess up, Maggie!"
[507] "Well!" Quinn said, looking at the villain and then at the girl. "Parsons, you're getting on in years and wiser. I didn't hear you wrong, did I? You two sat together? When? Why? How long were you together?"
[508] Maggie O'Connell's face turned red and then white. She glared at Johnny and then turned to face Detective Quinn. "I think I'll just tell you everything I know," she said, annoyed, "This idiot is just a coward. I'll tell you everything I know, sir. If you don't tell that bastard manager, God will bless you."
[509] "I see," Quinn said, lowering his eyebrows. "Young lady, if you had told me these things earlier, I could have saved a lot of time and trouble. Did you stand up during the entire second act?"
[510] "Well, I stood up a few times, I think," she answered cautiously, "But everything was normal, and the manager wasn't around, so I sat back down."
[511] "Did you notice anyone sitting next to Field when you stood up?"
[512] "No, sir. I didn't know he was there. No... no, I didn't look over there, I think."
[513] "So, I guess," Quinn continued coldly, "during the second act, you don't remember bringing anyone to the last row, the last seat next to you?"
[514] "No, sir. I didn't know he was there. No... no, I didn't look over there, I think."
[515] "So, I guess," Quinn continued coldly, "during the second act, you don't remember bringing anyone to the last row, the last seat next to you?"
[516] "I don't remember, sir... uh, I know I shouldn't have done that, but I didn't notice anything out of the ordinary all night." As the questions escalated, she became increasingly tense. She glanced at the "priest" again, but he was staring at the floor.
[517] "You've been very helpful, young lady," Quinn said, suddenly standing up. "You can go now."
[518] Just as she turned to leave, the villain glanced at her innocently, stepping over the carpet to follow her. Quinn gestured to the police officer, and the "priest" was dragged back to his original position.
[519] "Don't rush, Johnny," Quinn said coldly. "O'Connell! Turn around, girl, and try to look indifferent." The girl turned around, attempting to appear uninterested. "I won't mention anything to Mr. Ponzel right now, but I advise you to be careful and mind your words when talking to your superiors. Go on, and if I hear any more trouble from you, only God can help you."
[520] She chuckled, swaying out of the office.
[521] Quin quickly turned to the officer. "Arrest him," Quin pointed to the villain, his voice stern, "Take him back to the police headquarters."
[522] The officer saluted. The handcuffs flashed, and with a click, the "priest" stared blankly at the handcuffs on his wrists. Before he could say anything, he was pushed out of the office.
[523] Quin waved his hand dismissively and sat down in the leather chair, taking a pinch of snuff, and then spoke to Johnson in a completely different tone: "Could you please, Johnson, bring Mr. Morgan in?"
[524] Benjamin Morgan strode confidently into Quin's temporary office, but could not fully hide his inexplicable panic. He spoke in a pleasant, deep baritone, "Ah, sir, I'm here." He sat down in a chair, a look of satisfaction on his face, as if he had just sat down in a club meeting room after a long day. Detective Quin was not fooled by his appearance. He stared intently at Morgan, making the already gray-haired, overweight Morgan feel uneasy.
[525] "I'm Quin, Mr. Morgan," he said in a friendly tone, "Richard Quin, Detective."
[526] "I guess so," Morgan stood up and shook hands with Quinn, "I assume you know who I am, officer? Several years ago, I appeared in front of you more than once in the criminal court. There was a case, do you still remember? I was Mary Duluth's defense lawyer, she was on trial for murder…"
[527] "Yes, I remember!" Quinn exclaimed, "I was wondering where I had seen you before. If I'm not mistaken, you helped her clear her name. You did a great job, Morgan - very, very nice. It's you, isn't it? Good, good!"
[528] Morgan smiled. "That was a good job. I admit it," he said, "But, I'm afraid that's all in the past, sir. You know, I don't defend people in criminal court anymore."
[529] "Stopped?" Quinn took a pinch of snuff, "I didn't know. What happened?"
[530] Morgan remained silent. After a while, he crossed his legs and said, "There's a big problem. Can I smoke?" He suddenly asked. After getting Quinn's consent, he lit a thick cigar and was enveloped in the smoke.
[531] For a while, neither of them spoke. Morgan seemed to notice that Quinn was scrutinizing him, so he kept changing his posture, sometimes crossing his legs and sometimes putting them down, avoiding Quinn's gaze. The old man seemed to be pondering, with his head hanging on his chest.
[532] This silence was tense and awkward. The only sound in the room was the ticking of the grandfather clock in the corner. Suddenly, a voice could be heard from somewhere in the theater, raising its pitch, becoming a shout or a protest, and then falling silent.
[533] "Well, officer…" Morgan coughed. He was enveloped in the smoke of the cigar, his voice was a bit shrill and tense. "What's this… a fatigue interrogation?"
[534] Quinn looked up, surprised. "What? What did you say, Mr. Morgan? I think I was distracted. I keep bringing up that… Oh, I'm getting old!"
[535] "Mr. Morgan," Quinn asked him in his habitual jumping conversation, "do you know why I asked you to stay and chat with me?"
[536] "Ah, I don't know, sir. I suppose it's related to the case that happened tonight. But what does that have to do with me? I have to admit, I don't know what you're getting at." Morgan took a deep drag on his cigar.
[537] "Perhaps, Mr. Morgan, you'll find out soon," Quinn said, leaning back on the table. "Someone was killed here tonight – it wasn't an accident, I can assure you – the victim was a man named Monte Field."
[538] Quinn announced this with a calm tone, but it left Morgan stunned. He jumped up from his chair, his eyes wide, his hands trembling, his breath heavy, and his cigar fell to the ground. Quinn looked grim at him.
[539] "Monte – Field!" Morgan exclaimed, sounding terrified. He stared at Quinn's face, then sat down heavily on the chair, his whole body slumping.
[540] "Pick up your cigar, Mr. Morgan," Quinn said. "Mr. Pensler was so kind to lend me his office, I can't let his generosity go to waste." Morgan mechanically bent down and picked up the cigar.
[541] Quine thought to himself, "Either my friend is the world's best actor, or he's genuinely shocked out of his wits." He stood up straight. "Alright, Mr. Morgan, pull yourself together. Why does Field's death affect you so much?"
[542] "But... but, old chap! Monte Field... oh, my God!" He burst out laughing—a mad cackle that made Quine sit up straight with alarm. The laughter continued, and Morgan's body shook with hysteria. Quine, the detective, knew this symptom well, so he slapped Morgan and grabbed his collar, lifting him up.
[543] "Don't get carried away, Morgan!" Quine barked. This stern voice worked. Morgan stopped laughing, looked at Quine blankly, and sat back down—his body still trembling, but his demeanor now normal.
[544] "I... I'm really sorry, officer," he mumbled, wiping his face with a handkerchief. "It's... it's just shocking."
[545] "Clearly," Quine said coldly, "even if the earth opened up beneath your feet, you couldn't be more surprised. Now, Morgan, tell me what's going on."
[546] Morgan continued wiping the sweat from his face. His cheeks were red, and he trembled, hesitatingly biting his lips.
[547] "Alright, sir," he finally said, "what do you want to know?"
[548] "That's good," Quine approved. "So, when was the last time you saw Monty Field?"
[549] Morgan cleared his throat uneasily. "Er... I haven't seen him in a while," he whispered in response. "You see, we used to be partners - successful in legal matters. Then some things happened, and we went our separate ways. Since then, I... I haven't seen him again."
[550] "How long ago was that?"
[551] "About two years and a bit."
[552] "Very good." Quinn leaned forward. "I also want to know why you split up."
[553] Morgan looked down at the carpet, fiddling with his cigar. "I... uh, I guess you know what kind of person Field is. We had a disagreement about ethics, and after a small argument, we decided to go our separate ways."
[554] "Did you part on good terms?"
[555] "Uh... in that situation, yes."
[556] Quinn tapped the table. Morgan shifted uncomfortably. He clearly hadn't fully recovered from the shock.
[557] "What time did you arrive at the theater tonight, Morgan?" the officer asked.
[558] Morgan seemed surprised by the question. "Ah—around 8:15," he replied.
[559] "Let me see your ticket stub," Quinn said.
[560] Morgan fumbled through his pockets before producing it and handing it to Quinn. Quinn took the ticket stub, then pulled out three more from his pockets, placing them under the table and carefully comparing them. He then returned the four ticket stubs to his pocket without expression.
[561] "So, you were sitting in the middle M2 seat, right?" he said. "Nice spot, Morgan. What made you want to see 'Gunplay'?"
[562] "Ah, it's a novelty show, isn't it, officer?" Morgan looked a bit embarrassed. "I didn't even know I'd want to come to the theater – you know, I don't usually go to the theater – but the manager of the Roman Theater sent me a free ticket for tonight's performance, so I came."
[563] "Is that so?" Quine asked bluntly. "When did you receive the ticket?"
[564] "Ah, I got the letter on Saturday morning, officer, delivered to my office."
[565] "Oh, you got a letter too, did you? You don't happen to have it on you, do you?"
[566] "I—very—sure—I brought it," he mumbled, starting to rummage through his pockets, "Here it is! Here you go."
[567] He handed the officer a small rectangular piece of rough-edged paper made of wrinkled securities printing paper. Officer Quinn carefully took it to the light. Through the printed lines, a clear watermark pattern could be seen. He pursed his lips and carefully placed the paper on the desk's notepad. Under Morgan's gaze, he opened the drawer above Posen's desk and searched until he found a sheet of paper. The paper was large and square, with a luxurious theater seal printed on it. Quinn placed the two papers side by side, pondered for a moment, then sighed, picked up the one Morgan handed him, and read it slowly.
[568] The Roman Theater Department cordially invites Benjamin Morgan, Esq. to attend the performance of 'Gunfight' on September 24th (Monday) evening. We sincerely hope that Mr. Morgan, a leading figure in the legal profession in New York, will share his valuable opinions on the social and legal aspects of the play. Of course, we do not impose any obligation. The Roman Theater Department guarantees that Mr. Morgan can accept this invitation without any obligation.
[569] (Signature) Roman Theater
[570] Inviter: S.
[571] The "S" written in ink was sloppy and almost unrecognizable.
[572] Quin looked up, smiled. "Roman Theatre is quite something, Mr. Morgan. I was wondering -" he continued to smile, gesturing towards Johnson, who had been sitting quietly in the corner, listening to their conversation.
[573] "Call the manager, Johnson," Quin said, "if that publicity man - that Bellson, or Pearson, or whoever - is here, have him come too."
[574] After Johnson left, Quin turned back to Morgan.
[575] "Could you show me your gloves, Mr. Morgan?" he whispered.
[576] Morgan looked at him perplexed and then threw the gloves on the table in front of him. Quinn picked them up curiously. They were a pair of white silk gloves — typically worn with evening attire. Quinn pretended to be busy examining the gloves. He turned them over, closely inspecting a smudge on one fingertip, and even tried to put them on his own hand, joking with Morgan. After his examination, he returned the gloves to Morgan with a serious expression.
[577] "Ah — oh, by the way, Mr. Morgan — your hat is quite a sight. May I see it?"
[578] Morgan placed the hat on the table, still silent. Quinn picked it up nonchalantly, whistling a tune called "Broadway," a few notes lower than standard pitch. He flipped the hat in his hand. It was a high-quality hat, with a lustrous silk lining and a gold stamp of the manufacturer, "James Chalmers & Co." The initials "B.M." were embroidered on the band.
[579] Quinn grinned and put the hat on his own head. It fit perfectly. But he quickly took it off and handed it back to Morgan.
[580] "Thank you very much for allowing me to examine your gloves and hat, Mr. Morgan," he said as he pulled out a notepad from his pocket and jotted down a note hastily.
[581] The door opened, and Johnson, Pensel, and Harry Nelson entered. Pensel hesitated before approaching, while Nelson sat in an armchair.
[582] "What can I do for you, officer?" Pensel asked nervously, trying to ignore the dejected, silver-haired figure sitting in the chair.

[584] The manager's eyes widened. "Just one, officer. There's one right in front of you on the table."
[585] "Ah –" Quinn handed Pensel the paper he had taken from Morgan. "I want you to take a good look at this paper, Mr. Pensel. Do you know if the Roman Theatre has such paper?"
[586] The manager gave the paper a strange look. "No, I don't think we have such paper. In fact, I'm quite sure. What does it say?" He turned around and called out, "Nelson! What is this?—Is it your latest promotional gimmick?" He waved the paper in front of Nelson.
[587] Nelson took the paper from the manager and quickly read it. "Ah, it seems I'll have to change careers," he whispered. "This is bound to break the advertising record." He read it again, his face showing envy. Then, under the gaze of four reproachful eyes, he handed the paper back to Pencier. "I'm sorry, such a brilliant idea didn't even occur to me," he said slowly, retreating to the corner and crossing his arms.
[588] The manager turned to Quine with a puzzled expression. "This is strange, officer. As far as I know, the Roman Theater never uses such stationery, and I'm quite certain I never authorized anyone to do such promotional work. If Nelson says he didn't do it—"
[589] Quine carefully put the paper in his pocket. "Alright, thank you, gentlemen," he said, nodding at Pencier and Nelson as they left.
[590] He looked at Morgan, who was flushed with anger and embarrassment. Quine raised his hand and slammed it lightly on the table again.
[591] "What do you think of this, Mr. Morgan?" He asked straightforwardly.
[592] Morgan jumped up. "Damn it, this is a frame-up!" He waved his fist in front of Quinn, "Please excuse my rudeness, I know no more about this than you do. And don't think you can scare me by searching my hat and gloves - oh, you haven't searched my underwear yet, Inspector!"
[593] "But my dear Morgan," Inspector Quinn said gently, "why do you have to make yourself so nervous? Others would think I accused you of murdering Monte Field. Sit down, calm down, old boy. I'll ask you a simple question."
[594] Morgan slumped in the chair, wiped his forehead with a trembling hand, and whispered, "Sorry, Inspector, I couldn't control my temper. But these unfortunate things -" He calmed down and murmured.
[595] Inspector Quinn sat there, staring at him with a puzzled look. Morgan was busy wiping his sweat with a handkerchief and puffing on a cigar. Johnson coughed disapprovingly and looked up at the ceiling. Suddenly, there was a commotion in the room, but it soon quieted down.
[596] Quin's voice broke the silence. "Well, that's all, Mr. Morgan. You can go now."
[597] Morgan slowly rose, opened his mouth to say something, but closed it again, put on his hat, and left the room. Johnson saw the officer's gesture and lazily stood up, casually opening the door for him. Both men left.
[598] The room was left with Quin alone, who immediately got to work. He pulled out four ticket stubs, the letter Morgan had given him, and the lady's rhinestone handbag he had found in the dead man's pocket. He opened the handbag again, took out its contents, and laid them on the table in front of him. There were several business cards with "Frances Eves-Popp" printed elegantly; two delicate handkerchiefs; a small makeup case containing foundation, blush, and lipstick; a small money pouch containing twenty-dollar bills and some coins; and a door key. Quin toyed with these items for a moment, put them back in the handbag, and put the handbag, ticket stubs, and letter back in his pocket. He stood up, slowly scanning the room, then walked over to the coat rack, picked up a top hat hanging there, and checked its lining. It had the initials "L.P." printed on it, and the hat size was 6 3/4, which piqued his interest.
[599] He put the hat back in its place, opened the door.
[600] The four people sitting in the reception room immediately stood up, their faces expressing relief. Quin stood at the door, smiling, with his hands in his coat pockets.
[601] "It's finally your turn," he said. "Come on, everyone, come into the office."
[602] He politely stepped aside to let them go first – three ladies and a young man. They all entered the office with some excitement, the young man hurrying to pull chairs for the ladies to sit down. Four pairs of eyes eagerly looked at the elderly man standing at the door. Quein smiled, like a fatherly figure. He quickly glanced at the reception room, then closed the door, and sternly walked to the table and sat down, reaching for his snuffbox.
[603] "Alright!" he said mildly. "I'm very sorry to have kept you waiting so long – official business, you know. Now, let's get started. Well, right, right, I must – well, now, first of all, ladies and this gentleman, how shall we begin? I believe, young lady, although I haven't had the pleasure of hearing you introduce yourself, your name should be Frances Eves-Pope, is that right?"
[604] The girl raised her eyebrows. "Yes, sir," her voice was lively and melodious, "although I'm not sure how you know my name."
[605] She smiled. Her smile was attractive and seductive, and her gaze was captivating. She was in her prime, graceful and charming, with brown, large eyes and a complexion like jade. She exuded vitality that revitalized Quein's spirits.
[606] He smiled at her. "Well, Miss Eves-Pope, I think for most people, your identity would be a mystery. But I'm a policeman, so I'm stronger than most people, so it's not difficult for me to know your name. And you're definitely not an unknown girl, in fact, I saw your photo in the social section of the newspaper today."
[607] The girl smiled, a little nervous. "Oh, so that's how it is!" She said, "I'm starting to get scared. Sir, what do you want with me?"
[608] "Official business - always official business," Detective Quin said pitifully, "I'm interested in anyone, I always relate it to my profession... before asking you questions, I want to ask, who are your friends?"
[609] When Detective Quin looked at the three of them, all three coughed awkwardly. Miss Francis smiled charmingly. "I apologize, sir, please allow me to introduce my friends. These two ladies are Hilda Orangi and Eve Ellis, my good friends; this is Mr. Stephen Barry, my fiancé."
[610] Detective Quin looked at them with a touch of surprise. "If I'm not mistaken, you're all members of the cast of 'Gunfight', right?"
[611] All three nodded in unison.
[612] Queene turned to Francis. "I don't mean to pry, Miss Eves-Pope, but I want you to explain... why your friends are here with you?"
[613] The three actors stood up very awkwardly. Francis looked from her companions to Queene, pleading with her eyes.
[614] "I—I'm sorry, officer," she said quickly. "I... I've never been questioned by the police before. I'm nervous, and I want my fiancé and these two ladies—my closest friends—to be here with me when you ask me questions. I didn't realize it went against your wishes...”
[615] "I understand," said Queene, smiling. "I completely understand. But, you see—" he made a gesture for them to go out first.
[616] Stephen Barry leaned over the girl's chair, a challenging look in his eyes as he stared at Quinn. "Darling, I won't leave if you need me."
[617] "But, Stephen, dear—" Frances helplessly sobbed. Quinn's expression remained firm, showing no signs of relenting. "You...you should go out first. But please wait outside for me. It won't be long, right, sir?" she asked, her eyes showing displeasure.
[618] Quinn shook his head. "Not too long." His attitude had changed completely, seeming to become somewhat irritable. The other three seemed to sense his change, subtly aligning themselves against him.
[619] Hilda Orlandi was a tall, plump woman in her forties, still attractive despite her age, with traces of her youthful beauty on her face. Under the cold light in the room, her face lost its color. She leaned over Frances, staring at Detective Quinn with a firm tone in her voice. "We'll wait for you outside, dear, if you feel uncomfortable or anything, just call out, and we'll come right in to help you." She huffed out of the room, followed by Eve Ellis, who patted Frances' hand comfortingly. "Don't worry, Frances, we're all here for you." She tugged on Barry's arm and followed Hilda Orlandi out. Barry glanced back at Quinn, a mix of anger and concern in his eyes. He glared at Quinn again before slamming the door shut.
[620] Quinn immediately stood up, his movements clean and his attitude cold. He stared into Frances' eyes, his hand resting on the surface of the table. "Now, Frances Eves-Pope, Miss," he said succinctly, "I'd like to talk to you about this...” He reached into his pocket, pulling out a rhinestone purse like a stage magician. "I'd like to return your purse to you."
[621] Francis sat up a little, her gaze shifting from Detective Quinn to the sparkling handbag. Her face turned pale. "Oh, that's... that's my evening bag!"
[622] "Indeed, Miss Evans-Pope," Quinn said, "it was found at the theater—tonight."
[623] "Of course!" She smiled nervously and sat back down. "I'm such a fool! I didn't even know my bag was missing until now..."
[624] "But, Miss Evans-Pope," Detective Quinn continued solemnly, "where we found your bag is more important than finding the bag itself."
[625] Francis opened her mouth wide, staring at him with fear in her eyes. "Yes, I heard about it." She whispered.
[626] "Well, Miss Evans-Pope," Quinn coldly continued, "your bag was found in the pocket of the deceased!"
[627] Fear filled the girl's eyes. She let out a sob and swayed on her chair, her face pale and tense.
[628] Quinn leaped forward, a look of pity and concern on his face. He touched Francis's weak body just as the door burst open and Stephen Barry rushed into the room, his coat flapping behind him. Hilda Orlancy, Eve Ellis, and Johnson followed close behind.
[629] "What did you do to her, you damned detective!" Barry shouted, shoving Quinn aside. He gently held Francis in his arms, brushing aside the strand of black hair that was in front of her face and whispering comforting words in her ear. Francis sighed and looked up at the agitated face next to her, confused. "Stephen, I...I feel dizzy," she murmured, and then sank back into Stephen's embrace.
[630] "Bring some water!" the young man exclaimed, stroking her hand. Johnson immediately handed over a flat glass cup. Barry forced a few gulps down her throat, and Francis coughed and regained her senses. The two actresses pushed Barry aside and sternly ushered the men out of the room. Quinn followed the protesting young man and the detective out the door with a submissive expression.
[631] "You're a good cop, aren't you?" Barry sniped at Quinny. "What did you do to her – give her a whack on the head with your usual police tactics?"
[632] "Oh, oh, young man," Quinny said mildly, "don't talk so harshly. She was just frightened."
[633] They all stood there in silence, the tension palpable. The door opened, and the two actresses emerged, supporting Francis. Barry rushed to her side, stroking her hand and whispering, "Do you feel better now, dear?"
[634] "Stephen – take me – home," she gasped, leaning on Barry.
[635] Quinny stepped aside, letting them pass. He watched them slowly make their way to the front door, accompanied by a small crowd of spectators.
[636] Chapter Six: The District Attorney Becomes a Biographer
[637] Detective Richard Quinn was a unique man. Although small in stature, he was remarkably sturdy; his head was full of white hair, and his face was etched with lines that spoke of his rich life experiences. He could have been a corporate executive or a night watchman, depending on his whim. Of course, with the right clothes, his unremarkable build could pass for any profession.
[638] This adaptability was also evident in his demeanor. No one could truly understand him. He was a source of wonder for his colleagues, his enemies, and those wretches he handed over to the legal system. He could be any kind of personality, from mild-mannered to arrogant, from paternal to obstinate, depending on his whim.
[639] However, deep down, as some more emotional people would argue, Quinn had a "heart of gold." He had no malice in his heart, was warm-hearted, and was not affected by the cruel world. For those who dealt with him for business or official matters, he was a chameleon, and indeed he was. He always showed different aspects of his personality, as he found it good: people never understood him, never knew what he was going to say or do, so they always had a bit of fear of him.
[640] At this moment, he returned to Pensel's office alone, closed the door, paused the investigation, and returned to his true self. At this moment, his face was wrinkled - physically old, but mentally wise. The image of the frightened, terrified girl kept flashing in his mind. Seeing her frail, terrified face made him want to escape. Francis Eves-Pope embodied everything an aging person would want their daughter to have. Seeing her fiancé desperately defending her made him feel ashamed.
[641] He sighed, reached for his snuff box, and took a deep drag. Although he was usually thrifty, he was willing to spend money on this.
[642] There was a sudden knock at the door, and he immediately changed into an officer's demeanor - sitting at the desk, pondering the case. In fact, he hoped that Ellery would come in.
[643] He said warmly, "Come in." The door opened, and a thin, bright-eyed man in a heavy coat and a wool scarf walked in.
[644] "Henry!" the officer called, standing up. "I thought the doctor told you to stay in bed!"
[645] District Attorney Henry Sampson winked at Quinn and sat down in an armchair.
[646] "Doctor," he lectured, "Doctors are so annoying. Now, tell me about the case. How's it going?"
[647] He groaned, carefully feeling his throat. Detective Queen sat down.
[648] "For an adult, Henry," he said firmly, "you're the most uncooperative patient I've ever seen. You'll get pneumonia if you're not careful."
[649] "Well," the district attorney grinned, "I have insurance, so I should worry... Oh, you haven't answered my question yet."
[650] "Oh, yes," Queen grumbled, "your question. The case, you ask? It's going nowhere, my dear Henry. Is that satisfactory for you?"
[651] "Please be more detailed," Henry said, "remember, I'm a patient, my head is still buzzing."
[652] "Henry," Quine said, leaning forward solemnly, "I'll tell you, this case is the most difficult one we've ever encountered in our department...Your head is buzzing, isn't it? I don't even want to talk about my head."
[653] Henry frowned, looking at Detective Quine. "If it's really as you say—I think—this is not a good time. The election is coming soon, an unsolved murder case, if it falls into the hands of the opposition... "
[654] "Well, that's just one aspect," Quine whispered, "I haven't considered the case from the election perspective, Henry. Someone was killed—to be honest, I have no idea who killed him or how he was killed."
[655] "I accept your kind criticism, officer," Henry's tone lightened, "but if you knew what I heard just a few minutes ago—on the phone..."
[656] "Wait a moment, my dear Watson—as Ellery often says," Quin chuckled—his sudden change in demeanor was typical of him, "I bet I know what happened. You were at home, probably in bed. The phone rang, and then you heard complaints, protests, grumbling, and various sounds a person makes when agitated. The voice said: 'I can't believe I'm being treated like a criminal, being detained by the police. I want Quin to be severely punished! He's a serious threat to personal freedom!' and so on, right?"
[657] "My dear fellow!" Sampson smiled.
[658] "This gentleman, the one who complained to you,” the officer continued, “is a short, chubby man with gold-rimmed glasses, speaks like a woman, and has a very unpleasant voice. He also showed great concern for 'a very good friend, District Attorney Henry,' which is touching, isn't it?"
[659] Sampson sat there, staring at Quin. Then, a smile spread across his eager face.
[660] "It's shocking, my dear Sherlock!" he whispered, "Since you know so much about my friend, perhaps you can easily tell me his name?"
[661] "Ah - is it this guy, right?" Quine said, blushing, "I - Ellery, my child! Nice to see you."
[662] Ellery entered the room, shaking Sampson's hand warmly. Sampson greeted him enthusiastically, having worked with him for a long time. Ellery inquired about the District Attorney's condition and then set a large jar of coffee and a paper bag on the table, which could not help but make one think of French pastries inside.
[663] "Come on, gentlemen, the big search is finally over, done, even though the yield was small, the hardworking investigators now deserve a nightcap," he chuckled, patting his father's shoulder affectionately.
[664] "But, Ellery!" Quine exclaimed happily, "this is a great surprise! Henry, do you want to join us and celebrate a little?"
[665] "I don't know what you're celebrating, but count me in," Sampson said. The three then enjoyed their meal together.

[734] "Please follow my line of thinking... Since Flint's hat is missing and no other hat was found in his place, it's clear that the hat was taken, and that's a fact. Let's examine the first possibility. If he knew beforehand, we can logically assume that the killer should have brought another hat to the theater to replace Flint's hat, instead of taking the victim's hat directly, leaving a clear clue. Bringing a replacement hat would be safer. And it shouldn't be difficult for the killer to find such a hat, because if he knew its importance beforehand, he would have further investigated the dimensions, style, and other details of Flint's hat. But we don't find a replacement hat here. We have every reason to believe that such a well-planned case should have had a replacement hat. But it's not there, so our only conclusion is that the killer didn't know the importance of the hat beforehand; otherwise, he would have taken preventive measures and left a hat. That way, the police would never know the importance of Flint's hat.

[769] Part Two
[770] ... For example, one time, young Gene C came to me after a month of diligently investigating a difficult case. He looked desperate and silently handed me a piece of paper. After reading the contents, I was surprised to find that it was his resignation letter.
[771] "Hey, Gene!" I called out, "What's this all about?"
[772] "I failed, Mr. Brierly," he whispered, "A month's work gone to waste. My investigative approach was wrong, it's embarrassing."
[773] "Gene, my friend," I said solemnly, "You're quitting over this?" I tear his resignation letter into pieces, and he looks at me in shock. "Go ahead," I advise, "Start over. Remember the adage: One must know what is wrong before he knows what is right."
[774] - Excerpt from Augustin Brierly's memoirs, "Recollections of a Chief of Police"
[775] Chapter Eight: Quinn Father and Son Meet Field's Best Friend
[776] The Quinn's apartment is located on West 87th Street. From the pipe rack above the fireplace to the gleaming military swords on the wall, it is easy to see that this is a typical man's residence. The house is made of brown bricks and has been preserved from the Victorian era. There are three households living in it, and the Quinn family resides on the top floor. As you climb the stairs covered in thick carpeting, you walk through a seemingly endless, dimly lit rectangular corridor, and just when you think that only a mummy would live in such a gloomy place, you arrive at a massive oak door with the name "Quinns" written on it in neat handwriting, framed in a border. Then, Junna peeks out from behind the door, grinning at you, and you enter a whole new world.
[777] Not only one person has left their cozy nest to willingly climb these unpleasant stairs to seek refuge here. Not only one famous name has been unceremoniously taken from the doorway to the living room by Junna.
[778] In fact, the entrance hall was inspired by Ellery. It is small and narrow, so the surrounding walls appear very high and unnatural. One wall is completely covered by a tapestry depicting a hunting scene - a tapestry that is the most suitable decoration for this medieval-style room, conveying a sense of humor and solemnity. The Quinn family deeply dislikes this tapestry but still keeps it because it was a gift from a impulsive duke, expressing gratitude from the royal family, as Richard Quinn had helped the duke's son avoid a scandal, although the details have never been made public. Beneath the tapestry is a heavy pulpit table with a lambskin lamp and a pair of bronze bookends holding a three-volume set of "One Thousand and One Nights."
[779] Additionally, there are two pulpit chairs and a small carpet in the entrance hall.
[780] It is always dimly lit and unbearable here. However, when you pass through this oppressive place, you will see a pleasantly wide room that you would never expect. This stark contrast is also the humor of Ellery, and without him, the old man would have thrown the entrance hall and its furniture into some dark corner.
[781] The living room has bookshelves on three walls, emitting the smell of leather, stacked up to the ceiling. On the other wall, there is a huge primitive fireplace with a hard oak mantelpiece, with glowing ironwork as the grate dividing the fireplace. Above the fireplace, there is a famous crossbow knife, a gift from a swordsmanship coach in Nuremberg to Richard, as the young Richard lived with him during his studies in Germany. The room is brightly lit, illuminating the entire cluttered and spacious area, with armchairs, sofa, footstools, and brightly colored leather cushions everywhere. In other words, for two high-intelligence gentlemen with refined taste, this is the most comfortable room they could design. However, the room is cluttered and messy, and after a while, the air becomes foul. Juno, however, can prevent this from happening. He is a jack-of-all-trades, serving as a janitor, housekeeper, butler, personal servant, and the family's lucky charm, keeping the entire room in order.
[782] When Ellery was at university, the elder Quinn felt lonely, and Juno was adopted by Quinn during that time. He is a cheerful young man, nineteen years old, and has been an orphan since he can remember. He is always happy and has never realized that he needs a surname. He is slender and short, a bit timid, but very cheerful. Sometimes he is so excited that he dances around, and at other times, he is quiet as a mouse. This Juno worships the elder Quinn like an ancient Alaskan idolizing their totem. He has a very close relationship with Ellery, although both of them are not very talkative, but they can show it through the boy's attentive service. Juno lives in a small room next to the Quinn father and son's bedroom, and Richard once joked, "You can hear a flea singing to its mate in the middle of the night."

[1306] Chapter 13: The Quinns on the Quinns
[1307] At 6:30 that evening, after dinner, Juno had just cleared the table and was bringing coffee for the Quinns when the doorbell rang. [1313]“I've been looking forward to meeting our city's Paracelsus, the famous toxicologist.” Ellery smiled. “Your name sends shivers down the spines of New Yorkers.” He pretended to shudder and pointed to the chair next to him. The four sat down.
[1314]“Have some coffee, gentlemen,” Quinns suggested, then called out to Juno, who was peeking out from the kitchen doorway. “Juno! You little scamp! [1315]Plautti looked like the devil himself as he pulled a frightening-looking black cigar from his pocket and puffed away vigorously.
[1316] "Idle chatter is only suitable for people like you," he said after taking a puff of smoke, "But me, I spend my days analyzing the contents of a woman's stomach, just so I can go home and get some sleep."
[1317] "I know, I know!" Ellery muttered, "I learned from seeking your help from Dr. Jones that you encountered difficulties in analyzing Mr. Field's body. Tell me, my Esquilp."
[1318] "I will," Plautus replied gravely. "You're right - I encountered a major difficulty. Professionally speaking, I can humbly say that I have some experience in examining the organs of deceased ladies and gentlemen, but I admit I've never seen anyone's organs as messed up as this guy's. I mean it, Jones can vouch for that. For example, his esophagus and entire trachea looked like someone had sprayed them with a blowtorch."
[1319] "What - it couldn't be mercuric chloride, could it, doctor?" Ellery asked. He was proud of his limited knowledge of precise science.
[1320] "No, impossible," Plautus said in a low voice. "Let me tell you what happened. And let me tell you a secret - the coroner thought I was overworking myself and seeing things, so he tried it himself with that delicate Italian hand of his. The result, my friends, was zero. So, let's leave this problem to our fountain of knowledge and let him tell us his experience."
[1321] Dr. Sauls Jones cleared his throat ominously. "Thank you for the dramatic introduction, my friend," he said in a low voice. "Yes, the body was turned over to me. At this point, I must say that this is the most shocking discovery in the fifteen years of my career at the toxicology office."
[1322] "Good heavens, heavens!" Quinlow whispered, taking a pinch of snuff. "I'm beginning to admire the intelligence of our murderer's friend. Recent events suggest that this murder is anything but ordinary. What did you find, doctor?"
[1323] "I naturally assumed that Platt and the coroner had done a thorough job preparing," Dr. Jones began, crossing his bony legs, "and they usually do. So, I first analyzed this little-known poison. Little-known, I mean, from the criminal's point of view. To illustrate how thorough my search was—I even considered one of the backup poisons favored by our science fiction novelist friends: arrow poison, a toxin from South America. Five detective novels out of four use this poison. But even the most criticized arrow poison in its family disappointed me..."
[1324] Ellery leaned back and laughed. "If you're being sarcastic about my profession, Dr. Jones, I can tell you that none of my novels have used arrow poison."
[1325] The toxicologist's eyes lit up. "So, you're a detective novelist too, eh? Quinlow, old pal, let me express my condolences to you... Nevertheless, gentlemen, when it comes to explaining the rarity of the poison, we can usually draw a clear conclusion—namely, that it is a rare poison in the pharmacopoeia. Of course, we know very little about many rare poisons—especially Eastern drugs."
[1326] "Well, to put it briefly, I found myself in a dilemma that made me very unhappy." Dr. Jones recalled the situation and chuckled softly, "That conclusion was indeed unhappy. [1329] For a scientist, Dr. Jones' high-pitched announcement would have had a dramatic effect. But for the detective, it meant nothing. For Ellery, he just murmured, "Sounds like a mythical monster!"
[1330] Dr. Jones smiled slightly and continued, "Didn't you notice that? Let me tell you about tetraethyl lead. It has almost no smell – more precisely, its physical properties are like trichloromethane, that's the first point. Second – it has a taste – of course, it's weak – but it can be confirmed that it smells like ether. Third – it is very toxic and terrifying. It is very toxic – let me tell you what kind of damage this potent chemical can do to living tissue."
[1331] By now, the toxicologist had completely captured the attention of the audience.
[1332] "I took a healthy rabbit, the kind used for experiments, and painted undiluted tetraethyl lead on - notice, just a little - in the soft area behind the ear. Remember, this is not an internal injection, but only applied to the skin. It will be absorbed by the skin and then reach the blood. I observed the rabbit for an hour - after that, I didn't need to observe it anymore, it was dead, just like any other dead rabbit."
[1333] "I don't see it as very toxic, doctor," the officer questioned.
[1334] "Not strong? Well, believe me, it's extraordinary. It was just a matter of painting a little on a whole piece of healthy skin - I'll tell you, I was shocked. If there were a wound on the skin or if it was ingested internally, that would be another matter. So, you can imagine what would happen to Field after swallowing this stuff - he drank a lot of it!"
[1335] Ellery's eyebrows furrowed in thought. He began to wipe the lenses of his spectacles.
[1336] "This is not all," Dr. Jones continued. "As far as I know – I've been working in this city for years and I have some knowledge of developments in other parts of the world – but as far as I know, tetraethyl lead has never been used for criminal purposes."
[1337] The detective sat up straight, his eyes widening. "That's significant, doctor!" he murmured. "Are you sure?"
[1338] "Of course. That's why I'm so interested."
[1339] "How long would it take to kill someone with this poison, doctor?" Ellery asked slowly.
[1340] Dr. Jones looked grim. "I can't give you an exact answer because, as far as I know, no one has died from this poison before. But I can make a fairly accurate guess. I think Field would not have lasted more than fifteen minutes, maybe twenty, after ingesting this poison."
[1341] Quin's cough broke the ensuing silence. "On the other hand, doctor," he said, "isn't the uniqueness of this poison what makes it easy to trace? What do you think is its most common source? [1342] The toxicologist's face twisted into a bitter smile. "Tracking down this poison," he said earnestly, "is up to you. You can look it up. What I can confirm right now - remember, it's almost completely new to us - tetraethyl lead is most commonly found in a certain type of petroleum product. I stumbled upon the simplest method to produce it in large quantities. You won't guess how. It can be extracted from the most common and ordinary gasoline."
[1343] The Quin father and son exclaimed in a low voice, "Gasoline!" The detective shouted, "Ah - but how are we supposed to trace it?"
[1344] "The problem is here," the toxicologist replied. "I can go to a gas station on the corner, fill up a tank, drive home, and extract tetraethyl lead from the gas tank with a little time and effort."
[1345] "Does that mean, doctor," Ellery interjected hopefully, "that the killer needs some experience with chemical experiments - familiarity with chemical analysis, or something like that?"
[1346] "It doesn't say that. Anyone with a home distillation apparatus can extract tetraethyl lead, and leave no trace. The advantage of the process is that the boiling point of tetraethyl lead is higher than any other component in gasoline. All you have to do is extract the other components at a certain temperature, and what's left is this poison."
[1347] The officer took a pinch of snuff and his fingers trembled slightly. "I can only say – I'm in awe of this killer," he muttered. [1348] Dr. Jones snorted. "Officer, you surprise me. Your question has been answered already."
[1349] "What's going on? What do you mean?"
[1350] "I just told you how to extract it, didn't I? If you heard this from a toxicologist, and had a distillation room, wouldn't you be able to extract some too? [1359] "Oh!" Plaut immediately woke up. "The whiskey in the bottle is just like the other whiskeys I've tested - nothing wrong with it. Since the war, I haven't seen such high-quality alcohol in the country - except for those stored before the war... I think Wiley has already given you my report, and the ginger ale is also fine."
[1360] Quine nodded. "Well, that seems to be settled," he said gravely. "We seem to have made no progress on tetraethyl lead. You two are very familiar with it, and it will be more effective than other people. It's just a shot in the dark, and it may not yield any results."
[1361] "That's fine," Ellery whispered. "Every novelist has to write until the end."
