[1365] Ellery sat on the cushion, sighing. "Whenever I think about how all the investigations into human weaknesses are useless and a waste of time, my respected father puts such a responsibility on me again. Hey! What do you think?"
[1366] "I'm not putting any responsibility on you," Quine growled, "Stop exaggerating, I'm already dizzy. I want you to help me straighten out this mess of a case and see - uh, if we can find anything."
[1367] "I'm skeptical," Ellery said, "Where do I start?"
[1368] "You don't need to say anything," dad grumbled, "Tonight, I'll talk, you listen. You might want to take some notes.
[1369] "Let's start with Field. First, I think we can safely assume that our friend didn't go to the theater Monday night for entertainment, but for business. Agreed?"
[1370] "In my opinion, that's a given," Ellery said, "How did Willy report Field's activities on Monday?"
[1371] "Field went to the office at 9:30—he usually gets there in the morning. He worked until noon. There were no private visitors all morning. At 12:00, he had lunch alone at the Webster Club, returned to the office at 1:30, and worked until 4:00—he seemed to go straight home after work, as the doorman and elevator operator can confirm he was back in his apartment at 4:30. Michaels went to him at 5:00 and left at 6:00, and Willy found no other information. Field left the apartment at 7:30, dressed as we found him. I have a list of clients he saw that day, but it's not very useful."
[1372] "What's the reason for his low bank account balance?" Ellery asked.
[1373] "As I suspected," Quinn answered, "Field has been losing a lot of money in the stock market—not to mention he also wastes money at the racetrack. For a sensible man, he's a gullible fool. Anyway, that explains why his personal account cash is so low. Moreover, perhaps it can more firmly indicate that the '50,000' we found on the promotional material refers to money. And this money, I'm sure, is somehow related to the person he was supposed to meet at the theater."
[1374] "Now, I think we can safely conclude that Field is very familiar with the killer. On the one hand, he accepted the drink offered to him without the slightest suspicion, not even asking; on the other hand, they arranged to meet in a place that seemed to be for concealment—otherwise, why choose the theater?"
[1375] "Well, I'll ask you the same question," Ellery interrupted him, pursing his lips, "Why choose the theater as the meeting place? It's obvious that they were going to engage in a secret, criminal transaction. Why not the park, which is more concealed? Or the hotel lobby, which is also good? How do you answer that?"
[1376] "Unfortunately, my son," the detective said mildly, "Field didn't suspect anyone was going to kill him. In his mind, he just had to do his part in the transaction. In fact, it's likely that Field chose the theater as the meeting place himself. Perhaps he wanted to create an alibi for himself. We can't know for sure what he was trying to do. As for the hotel lobby - he would have risked being seen by others. And perhaps he didn't want to go to a secluded place like the park. Finally, he may have had a specific reason not to be seen with the other person. Don't forget, the ticket stubs we found show that the other person didn't go into the theater with Field. But these are all pointless speculations -"
[1377] Ellery smiled thoughtfully but said nothing. He thought to himself that the old man hadn't fully answered his question, and for a detective like Quin, who was more direct in his thinking, this was unusual...
[1378] But Quin continued speaking. "Well, we must always remember that the person who Field was dealing with may not be the killer. Of course, this is just one possibility. This crime seems to have been well-planned. But if that person isn't the killer, we need to find two people in the audience on Monday night who have a direct connection to Field's death."
[1379] "Morgan?" Ellery asked casually.
[1380] The detective shrugged. "Perhaps. Why didn't he tell us that when we spoke to him yesterday afternoon? He confessed to everything else. Well, maybe he felt that paying a blackmail fee to a murdered man and being at the theater was enough indirect evidence."
[1381] "Let's look at this," Ellery said. "We found a number '5,000' written on the flyer by the deceased, which obviously refers to money. Through Sampson and Cronin, we know that Field was a despicable man and possibly a criminal. Moreover, we learned from Morgan that he was good at extortion. Therefore, I think we can safely assume that he went to the Roman Theater on Monday night to collect or arrange the payment of the $50,000 ransom from someone we don't know yet. So far, it all makes sense, doesn't it?"
[1382] "Continue," the inspector grumbled without expressing his opinion.
[1383] "Okay," Ellery continued, "if we assume that the person who was being extorted and the murderer are the same person, we don't need to look for a motive anymore. The motive is clear - to stop Field from continuing to extort. However, if we assume that the murderer and the person being extorted are two different people, we have to continue looking for his motive. My view is that there is no need to do so - the murderer and the person being extorted are the same. What do you think?"
[1384] "I tend to agree with you, Ellery," the inspector said. "I only mentioned another possibility - without reaching a conclusion. So, let's continue on the assumption that the murderer and the person being extorted are the same..."
[1385] "Now - I want to find out about the missing tickets."
[1386] "Ah – missing tickets," Ellery murmured. "I wonder what you can make of that?"
[1387] "Be serious now, you little rascal!" Quin shouted. "That's all I know. Overall, we have to deal with eight seats – one of which belongs to Field, and we found the stub on him; one belongs to the killer, and Flint found the stub for us; and finally, there are six empty seats, with tickets sold according to the ticket office records, but nowhere in the theater or the ticket office were the stubs found, either torn or intact – nothing at all. First, it's highly unlikely that all six tickets were in the theater on Monday evening, and then someone took them out. Remember, the search of everyone that night wasn't very thorough, and it's possible that we overlooked something small like a ticket. However, that's basically impossible. The best explanation is that either Field or the killer bought eight tickets, intending to use two and leave the other six, ensuring that they could not be disturbed during their brief transaction. In this case, the wisest course of action would be to destroy the tickets as soon as they were purchased. It could have been done by either Field or the killer, depending on who arranged the meeting. So, we must forget about those six tickets – they no longer exist, and we'll never find them.
[1388] "Next," the detective continued, "we learned that Field and the person he was extorting entered the theater separately. This can be inferred from the fact that when I put the two stubs together, the tear didn't line up. If two people entered the theater together, the tickets would be handed to the usher together, and they would be torn together. This doesn't mean they didn't enter the theater at the same time; they may have come in one after the other for safety, pretending not to know each other. However, Maggie O'Connell claimed that no one sat in LL30 during the first act, and Jesse Lynch, the boy selling oranges, confirmed that no one sat there for at least ten minutes after the second act began. That means either the killer hadn't entered the theater yet or he had come in but sat somewhere else – he bought another ticket. "
[1389] Ellery shook his head.
[1390] "I realized that too, son," the old man said, a little irritated. "I was just working through the thought process. What I meant to say was that it seems highly unlikely that the killer entered the theater at the time of the performance; he probably came in after the second act started and at least ten minutes later."
[1391] "I can prove that," Ellery said lazily.
[1392] The officer took a pinch of snuff. "I know - the mysterious numbers on the flyers. What do you make of them?"
[1393] 930
[1394] 815
[1395] 50,000
[1396] "We know what 50,000 represents. The other two numbers, however, do not refer to money but to time. First, '815' – the play started at 8:25. It is likely that Field arrived around 8:15 or even earlier, as he glanced at his watch for some reason. Now, let's assume that he had an appointment with someone, but that person was late. It is very likely that Field jotted down the numbers on the flyer casually – first, '50,000', indicating the transaction he was considering, including the $50,000 ransom; then 8:15, the time he was considering the matter; and finally 9:30 – the time the victim should have arrived! Writing these things down was natural for Field, as anyone with a habit of scribbling in their spare time would do so. We are lucky, as this indicates two things: first, the exact time Field met with the killer was 9:30; second, it corresponds to the time we hypothesize the murder took place. At 9:25, Lynch saw Field alive and alone; by 9:30, according to what Field wrote, the killer should have arrived, and we naturally assume the killer did arrive at the theater; and according to Dr. Jones, the poison that killed Field took 15 to 20 minutes to take effect – considering that Paskack found the body at 9:55, we can infer that the poison was taken at 9:35. If the tetrachloroethylene took 20 minutes to take effect – that would be 9:55. Of course, the killer had already left the crime scene by then. Don't forget, he couldn't have known that Mr. Paskack would suddenly stand up and leave his seat. The killer may have thought that Field's body would not be discovered until intermission, at 10:05, so Field would have been dead and unable to leave any further information. The killer was undoubtedly lucky, as he discovered Field too late and did not leave any other information, only stating that he was murdered. If Paskack had left five minutes earlier, we would have caught the cunning killer by now."
[1397] "Great!" Ellery murmured, his smile full of affection, "That was well stated, congratulations!"
[1398] "Oh, go to hell," his father barked, "I was just repeating what you said Monday night in Penczel's office – in fact, although the killer left the crime scene between 9:30 and 9:55, he was still in the theater for the rest of the evening, until we got everyone to leave. Through your investigation of the night watchman and O'Connell, along with the night watchman's testimony, Jesse Lynch was in the alley, the usher confirmed this fact, and everything else … ponder on it, the killer was in the theater, without a doubt."
[1399] "This puts us in a temporary bind. Right now, all we can do is think about the character of the people we encountered during our investigation," the policeman sighed and continued, "First – Maggie O'Connell said she didn't see anyone walking in the aisle during the second act, did she tell the truth? And she said she didn't see anyone sitting in seat LL30 all night, but we know someone should have been there from 9:30 to the ten minutes or fifteen minutes before the body was found, so was she lying?"
[1400] "That's a tricky question, Dad." Ellery said solemnly, "Because if she was lying, we lose a lot of clues. If she was lying then – God help us – she could describe or even identify, or possibly even name the killer! However, her nervousness and strange demeanor may be due to the fact that she knows that Johnny was in the theater at the time and that many police officers are eager to catch him."
[1401] "It sounds reasonable," Quinn mumbled. "Well, what about Parsons Johnny? How did he get involved in this—or was he not involved at all? We must remember, according to Morgan, that Cazzanelli and Field had a close relationship. Field was his lawyer, and perhaps he bribed Parsons to help with the irregular transactions Croaning has been investigating. If Parsons being at the theater wasn't a coincidence, was it through Field or O'Connell? Is it as they both claimed? I think, my son," he tugged at his beard, adding, "I want to give Parsons Johnny a taste of the whip—it won't hurt his thick skin! And that little proud O'Connell girl—scaring her won't do much harm either..."
[1402] He took a deep breath of snuff, followed by a series of sneezes, and Ellery's sympathetic laughter accompanied him.
[1403] "And dear old Benjamin Morgan," the officer continued, "the anonymous letter conveniently provided him with a ticket, the source of which was very mysterious. Is he lying about this?"
[1404] "And then there's the most interesting lady, Mrs. Angela Roxboro... Ah! These ladies, bless them! They always muddle up a man's logic. What did she say—that she'd be at Field's flat by nine-thirty? That her absence was evidence? Of course, the porter confirmed her story. But it's easy to fix a porter... For Field's business—especially his private business—does she know more than she's letting on? Was she lying when she said Field told her he'd be back at the flat at ten? Remember, we know the meeting at the Roman Theater was at nine-thirty—did he really think he could be back home by ten? By taxi, it would take fifteen or twenty minutes on the road, leaving only ten minutes for the transaction—perhaps, of course. The subway wouldn't be much faster. We must remember, this woman didn't show up at the theater all evening."
[1405] "Just this beautiful woman alone will keep you busy," Ellery said. "It's obvious that she's hiding something. Have you noticed her brazen contempt? It's not just bluffing. She knows something, Dad. I'll be watching her closely - she'll slip up eventually."
[1406] "Haggstrom will take care of her." Quinn said absent-mindedly, "Now, what about Michaels? He doesn't have an alibi for Monday night. But that doesn't matter. He wasn't at the theater... Did he really go to Field's apartment on Tuesday morning to look for something? We've thoroughly searched Field's apartment—did we miss something? He lied about the check thing, and obviously didn't know Field was dead. He must have realized he was taking a risk coming to Field's apartment. He read the paper, he wouldn't want the police there. So, what's his reason for taking the risk?"
[1407] "Maybe something related to his prison term—I mentioned that, and he looked surprised, right?" Ellery chuckled.
[1408] "Maybe." The detective replied, "By the way, I found out from Weil about Michaels' stint in Elmira. Thomas reported it was a hush-hush case—much heavier than the light sentence he got in the reformatory. Michaels had been suspected of forgery—it looked serious for him. Then his lawyer, Field, cleverly defended him, and he was charged with a completely different offense—something like a minor theft—and that was the end of the forgery charges. This Michaels seems to have some pull—we have to keep a close eye on him."
[1409] "I have a little theory about Michaels," Ellery said thoughtfully, "but let's put that aside for now."
[1410] Quinn seemed not to have heard Ellery. He stared into the crackling fire in the hearth. "And Lune," he said, "a man like Lune, so closely connected to his employer, claiming he knows nothing else besides what his employer mentions, seems too hard to believe. Is he hiding something? If so, he'd better pray—because Croaning will soon make him reveal the truth."
[1411] "I like Croning," Ellery said, "How does a man muster the determination to do something like that... Have you thought of a question? I want to know if Morgan knew Angela Roxo. Despite both denying it, wouldn't it be interesting if they did?"
[1412] "Son," Quinn complained, "stop worrying yourself. We have enough on our plate already, so... Ah!"
[1413] The room was quiet and comfortable. Quinn lay down, the flickering firelight illuminating him. Ellery munched on a pastry. Juno sat in a corner, eyes shining with excitement, her small body hunched as she listened to their conversation.
[1414] Suddenly, in a moment of mental shift, the old man's gaze met Ellery's.
[1415] "Hats...," Quinn murmured, "We always come back to the hat."
[1416] Ellery's gaze was puzzled. "Back to the hat is fine, Dad. Hat - hat - hat! What does the hat have to do with the case? How much do we know about the hat?"
[1417] The officer shifted in his chair. He propped his feet up, took a pinch of snuff, and continued the discussion with renewed vigor. "Well. We can't be lazy about that damned silk hat. So far, what do we know? First, the hat is still in the theater. Isn't that interesting? After such a thorough search, we couldn't find any trace, which seems impossible... Everyone left, and there was nothing in the cloakroom; there was nothing found in the debris, nothing to suggest that the hat was torn or burned; in fact, there was no clue, no evidence to help us continue the investigation. Therefore, Ellery, in this regard, the only reasonable judgment is that we are looking in the wrong place! Further, wherever it is, it must still be in the theater, because we have been closed since Monday evening. Ellery, we will go back to the theater tomorrow and turn it upside down. Unless we see some light, I won't sleep."
[1418] Ellery was silent for a moment. "I'm not satisfied with what you said, Dad," he said finally, "Hat - hat - something is wrong!"
[1419] The old man nodded vigorously. "Since yesterday morning, when I had time to think about the hat, I felt that we were stuck somewhere. It's already Wednesday evening, and there's no progress. We've done all the necessary work, but we've got nothing... I stared into the fire in the fireplace, "Everything is a mess. I know all the ins and outs, but for some reason, I can't seem to put them together - explain everything... Son, the missing piece is the key to the whole case."
[1420] The phone rang. The officer jumped up to answer the phone. He listened carefully to a man's slow speech, commented lightly, and then hung up the phone.
[1421] "It's deep in the middle of the night, who is it? And you talk so much. Oh, you know a lot of secrets, right?" Ellery grinned.
[1422] "It's Edmund Krueger." Quin said, "You remember, I told him to go check the Roman Theater yesterday morning. He has been there for two days, yesterday and today. And he clearly reported that there was no secret hiding place in the theater. Edmund Krueger has the final say on such architectural issues, and if he says there's no hiding place, you can be sure there won't be any."
[1423] He stood up and saw Junna squatting in the corner. "Junna! Make the bed." He yelled. Junna quietly walked through the room and left with a silent smile. Quin turned to Ellery, who had taken off his coat and was fumbling with his tie.
[1424] "The first thing we have to do tomorrow morning is go to the Roman Theater and search again." The old man spoke firmly, "Tell you, son – I hate wasting time! Some people had better watch out!"
[1425] Ellery wrapped his father's shoulders with his muscular arm affectionately. "Go to sleep, you old cheat!" He laughed.

[1427] Part Three
[1428] "Great detectives are born, not made. Like all geniuses, they do not come from the meticulously cultivated police, but from the whole of humanity. The most amazing detective I have ever known is an old, dirty shaman who has never left the jungle... This truly outstanding detective has a unique gift — extraordinary observation of events, understanding of human thinking, and insight into the human mind. And this gift is the factor that solves the problem, or the unshakable law."
[1429] — Excerpt from "The Hunter's Guide" by James Redix, Jr.
[1430] Chapter 14: The Hat Problem Grows Larger
[1431] On Thursday, September 27, the day after the murder at the Roman Theater, Inspector Queequeg and Ellery got up early and hastily dressed. Juno was dragged out of bed and dressed in plain clothes, looking like a maid in the Queequeg household. They gobbled down a hurried breakfast in Juno's disapproving gaze.
[1432] They chewed their tasteless pancakes loudly, and the old man asked Juno to put through a call to Louis Pensel. After a while, the inspector spoke kindly into the phone: "Good morning, Pensel. I apologize for waking you up so early... Something important has happened, and we need your help. Can you come to the Roman Theater to open it for us? I told you that the theater would not be closed for long, and now it seems that you can still take advantage of the public's attention to this incident. I can't determine when we'll be able to reopen, but there's a glimmer of hope that it could be tonight. Can you lend me a hand?"
[1433] Pensel mumbled a comforting phrase sleepily.
[1434] "Can you come to the Roman Theater right away to open it for us?" the old man continued, "I told you that the theater wouldn't be closed for long, and now it seems that you can still take advantage of the public's attention to this incident. You know, I can't determine when we'll be able to reopen, but there's a glimmer of hope that it could be tonight. Can you lend me a hand?"
[1435] "Great!" Pensel's voice trembled with excitement over the phone line. "You want me to come to the theater right away? I'll be there in half an hour – I'm not even dressed yet."
[1436] "Alright," Quin replied. "Of course, Pencier – don't let anyone in yet. Wait for us on the sidewalk before you unlock the door, and don't tell anyone. We'll discuss it further inside the theater... Wait a moment."
[1437] He held the phone to his chest and looked at Ellery with a questioning gaze. Ellery gestured vigorously, and Quin mouthed a name, nodding in agreement before returning to the phone.
[1438] "Now there's one more thing I need your help with, Pencier," he continued. "Can you find Mrs. Philip, the enthusiastic lady? I'd like her to meet us at the theater as soon as possible."
[1439] "Certainly, Inspector," Pencier said. The inspector hung up the phone.
[1440] "Well, that's settled," he said, rubbing his hands together and reaching into his pocket for a snuff box. "Ah – ah – sneeze! Thank you, Sir Walter and all the hardworking pioneers who defended the tobacco industry!" He sneezed happily. "We'll be there in a moment, Ellery."
[1441] He picked up the phone again and issued a few cheerful orders to the detective headquarters before slamming the phone back onto the table and urging Ellery to put on his coat. Juno looked mournfully at them as they left. He often begged the officer to let him accompany them on their occasional trips to the secluded paths of New York, but the officer had his own ideas about raising teenagers and always refused. Juno regarded his benefactor as if he were a totemic object from the Stone Age, so he had to accept this inevitable outcome and hope for better luck in the future.
[1442] It was a cold, damp day. Ellery and his father pulled up their coat collars and walked towards Broadway and the subway. Both were unusually quiet, but their faces expressed a sense of eager anticipation - an uncanny similarity, yet so different - that promised to be an exciting and meaningful day.
[1443] They walked briskly along 47th Street towards the Roman Theater. The morning wind was biting, and the streets of Broadway and its spiderweb-like alleys were deserted. A man in a brown coat loitered in front of the closed glass doors of the lobby; another leaned comfortably against the high iron grille separating the left alley from the street. In front of the theater's main entrance, they could see the short, stout Louis Pincus standing and talking to Flint.
[1444] Pincus shook hands enthusiastically. "Well, the ban is finally lifted... it's so nice to hear the news, officer," he said.
[1445] Pincus fished out a large key ring and unlocked the lobby door. The four of them filed in. The dark-skinned manager fumbled with the lock on the inner door and then pushed it open. The darkened main hall lay before them.
[1446] Ellery shuddered. "Perhaps only the Metropolitan Opera House and Tess's grave are more lifeless than this theatre. It's the perfect place for a deceased relative."
[1447] The officer wasn't as verbose. He grumbled and pushed his son into the darkened auditorium. "Cut it out! You're making us nervous."
[1448] Pincus quickly stepped forward and turned on the main power switch. Under the glare of the giant arc lights and chandeliers, the auditorium took on its familiar contours. Ellery's fanciful metaphor was not as bizarre as his father had suggested. Filthy waterproof canvas covered the rows of long seats; shadows fell on the already dusty carpet; the white plaster wall behind the empty stage was bare, forming an ugly blotch amidst the red plush.
[1449] "I apologize for the waterproof canvas," the officer murmured to Pincus, "because we have to roll them up. We're going to conduct a small-scale search of the auditorium front. Flint, please bring in the two men outside. They might as well make some extra money."
[1450] Flint hurried out and returned with the two detectives waiting outside the theatre. Under the officer's instructions, they began to pull the large coverings off the rubber chairs, revealing rows of padded seats. Ellery stood near the left aisle, pulling out a small notebook from his pocket, which he had used to sketch the theatre layout on Monday night. He pondered silently, biting his lower lip. He occasionally looked up, as if to verify the layout of the theatre.
[1451] Quin quickly returned to the back of the theater, where Poulson was pacing nervously. "Poulson, we're going to be here for hours. I didn't bring enough people. Can you help me... I need to think about something... just take up a little of your time, but it'll be very helpful to me."
[1452] "Of course, Officer!" the short manager replied. "I'll be happy to help."
[1453] The officer coughed. "Don't think I'm treating you like a runner or anything, old pal," he explained apologetically. "But I need someone who's trained in searching for things like this... and I need some important information from a few people in the district attorney's office who are investigating other aspects of the case. Can you help me deliver a note to a man named Cronin... and bring back a package he gives you? I'm sorry to ask you to do this, Poulson, but it's too important for me to trust it to just any messenger, and—I'm in a bind."
[1454] Poulson smiled brightly. "Don't worry about it, Officer. If you need a note, I have paper and pen in my office."
[1455] The two returned to Poulson's office. Five minutes later, they returned to the auditorium. Poulson hurried out onto the street, holding a sealed letter. Quin watched him leave, sighed, and turned to Ellery. Ellery sat on the armrest of the chair where Field had been killed, still contemplating the pencil sketches he had made.
[1456] The officer whispered something to his son. Ellery smiled and patted the older man on the back.
[1457] "Son, shall we take action?" Quick said. "I forgot to ask Pencier if he notified Mrs. Philip. I guess he did, or he would have said so. Where is she anyway?"
[1458] Flint was busy removing the waterproof canvas cover with two other detectives, and Quick motioned for him to come over.
[1459] "I told you to do some stretching this morning, Flint. Go up and get busy."
[1460] "What am I looking for today, Officer?" the burly detective grinned. "Because I hope my luck is better than Monday night."
[1461] "You're looking for a hat - a fancy, shiny hat, the kind a stylish person wears, son," the officer said solemnly. "But if you see anything else, call me out loud!"
[1462] Ellery reluctantly took off his coat and slipped the pamphlet into his pocket. The officer twisted and turned to remove his overcoat, leading his son into the corridor. They began to search the audience together, slowly and methodically. They lifted the seats; the officer surreptitiously took out a long needle from his chest pocket, tentatively poking it into the plush cushion, then knelt down to examine every inch of the carpet with his flashlight.
[1463] The two detectives had rolled up the waterproof canvas cover and, under the officer's brief instructions, began to search the boxes, each responsible for one side of the theater.
[1464] After a while, the four of them were working in silence, only the officer's labored breathing breaking the stillness. Ellery's work was very efficient, while the old man was slower. After completing a row of search, they met in the middle, exchanged meaningful glances, shook their heads, and continued.
[1465] After Poulzer left about twenty minutes ago, the focused officers and Ellery were startled by the ringing of the phone. The sharp and clear ring echoed through the quiet theater. The father and son looked at each other blankly for a moment, then the old man chuckled and hobbled towards Poulzer's office.
[1466] Soon, he returned with a smile. "It's Poulson," he said. "He went to Field's office, but it was locked. No wonder – it's only 8:45. But I told him to wait until Cronin comes. Won't be long now."
[1467] Ellery chuckled, and they resumed their work.
[1468] Fifteen minutes later, just as the two were about to finish, the front door opened, and a short, middle-aged woman in black stood blinking in the bright arc lights. The officer stood up and went to greet her.
[1469] "Are you Mrs. Poulson?" he called out kindly. "It's so nice of you to come so quickly, ma'am. I'm sure you know me – I'm Quinn."
[1470] Ellery stepped forward with a rare smile, bowing politely and sincerely. Mrs. Poulson was a likable old woman, short and kind-looking, with silver hair and a friendly demeanor. She immediately won over Quinn's affection for her charming middle-aged appearance, and he had a fondness for mature ladies.
[1471] "I know Mr. Quine," she said, extending her hand. "He was very kind to an old lady on Monday night... I was afraid you'd have to wait for me, sir!" She turned to the officer and said softly, "Mr. Poulson sent a message this morning - you know I don't have a phone. I used to be an actress... so I came as soon as I could."
[1472] The officer smiled broadly. "That's quite a feat for a lady, Mrs. Philip," he said.
[1473] "My father kissed the ground at the feet of the Sphinx, Mrs. Philip," Ellery said gravely. "Don't believe a word he says... I think you'll be able to handle the rest of the front row, Dad? I'd like to chat with Mrs. Philip for a while. Will you be all right with this work alone?"
[1474] "Of course I will," the officer snorted. "Leave the aisle open, son... Thank you very much for your help, Mrs. Philip."
[1475] The silver-haired lady smiled. Ellery took her arm and led her to the stage. Quine watched them go, enviously, then shrugged and went back to searching. Soon, when he straightened up, he saw Ellery and Mrs. Philip talking earnestly on the stage, like two actors rehearsing. Quine continued to slowly walk between the rows of seats, looking for clues, but he was still empty-handed when he looked up again. The stage was empty, and Ellery and the old lady were nowhere to be found.
[1476] Detective Quinn finally arrived at seat LL32 on the left - it was here that Monte Field met his untimely demise. He carefully examined the cushion, his eyes full of disappointment. He slowly walked through the carpet at the back of the theater, entered Pincus's office. He reappeared shortly, heading towards Harry Nelson's cubicle, the publicity manager. [1620] Wiley jumped up like a soldier and said, "Well, I guess that's settled, officer!"

[1624] Chapter 16: The Quinns Go to the Theater

[1649] At exactly six o'clock, Quin and Ellery met Benjamin Morgan in the cozy lobby of Carlos Restaurant. He sat dejectedly in a red leather chair, staring at his hand; his lips drooped with worry, and his legs were subconsciously spread apart due to his gloomy mood.

[1669] "Yes, that's why I said it. He gave the impression that he was short of money and wanted to take a vacation—a trip to Europe for at least three years—he was soliciting funds from 'friends.' I didn't know he was blackmailing on a large scale, but this time—"

[1692] As the Quinn father and sons strolled down Broadway along Forty-Seventh Street, the sidewalk in front of the Roman Theater was packed with people. The crowd surged, and a barricade was set up to maintain order. The entire narrow passage was at a standstill. The strong lights above the marquee flashed the play's title, "The Gunfight," with smaller lights illuminating the cast list: starring James Parrish, Eve Ellis, and a full cast of stars. Men and women elbowed their way through the throng, police shouted for people to show their tickets to pass the barricade.
[1693] The officer flashed his badge, and he and Ellery were pushed and shoved along with the throng of spectators into the theater's small foyer. [1694] Poulson spotted the Quinn father and sons and approached them. Seeing the officer's proud demeanor, he hesitated, nodding knowingly, and returned to the cash window. [1696] The auditorium was packed. Crowds of people lined the aisles, being ushered quickly to their seats by ushers. Many people turned to look at the Quin father and son, who had inadvertently become the center of attention.

[1725] Chapter 17: More Hats
[1726] "Please sit down, Tim - would you like some coffee?"
[1727] Timothy Corning was sharp-eyed, medium-sized, and had a thick, unruly mane of fiery red hair. He sat in a comfortable chair in the Quine's home, slightly embarrassed by the officer's invitation.
[1728] It was Friday morning, and the officer and Ellery were dressed in colorful, romantic-looking pajamas; they had gone to bed early the night before - unusually early for them - and slept soundly; now Juno served a pot of steaming hot coffee, which he had brewed himself; everything seemed so perfect. Croning's abrupt entrance into the Quine's pleasant home - disheveled, melancholy, and openly cursing - was in stark contrast to the scene. The officer's polite protests could not stop the barrage of profanity that spewed from Croning's mouth; as for Ellery, he listened intently and attentively to the legal expert's words, as if an amateur were listening to a professional's advice.
[1729] Croning then realized his surroundings and blushed. He sat down after being invited, watching Juno deftly set the table for breakfast with a nimble hand.
[1730] "Your words are shocking, but I don't think you're in the mood to apologize, Tim Corning," the officer chided, crossing his hands behind his neck in a Buddha-like pose. "Should I ask you why you're upset, young man?"
[1731] "No need," Cropping gruffly said, restlessly moving his feet on the carpet, "You should have guessed. I ran into a dead end when I was looking for Field's files, damn it!"
[1732] "Damn it, Tim - damn it, don't be afraid," Quinn said sadly, "Poor Field is probably roasting his toes on a hissing coal fire in hell right now - laughing at you for blasphemy. What's the matter - what's the situation?"
[1733] Cropping grabbed the cup Juno had placed in front of him and drained the scalding coffee. "What's the matter?" he yelled, slamming down the cup, "Nothing - nothing! I swear, if I don't get the files soon, I'll go crazy! Hey - unbelievable. I dare swear that only God knows where Field hid the files, waiting for someone to take them away."
[1734] "You seem to have a phobia about hidden files, Cropping," Ellery said mildly, "People would think we still live in the reign of Charles I. There's no such thing as hidden files. You just need to know where to look."
[1735] Cropping grinned rudely. "Thank you very much, Mr. Quinn. It seems you're hinting at where Monty Field hid his files."
[1736] Ellery lit a cigarette. "Well. I accept the challenge… You don't doubt what you've said – you're sure the files exist, but they're not in Field's office… Why are you so sure that Field would keep files involving such a large gang? "
[1737] "He must have kept them," Croining retorted, "odd logic, but it works… My information confirms: Field had connections with high-ranking figures in the underworld and wrote plans related to them, which we've been trying to catch but haven't managed to encounter. Believe me; this case is too complicated to investigate thoroughly. But remember my words, Mr. Quin – Field has files he dare not destroy. Those are the files I'm looking for."
[1738] "Right," Ellery countered, "I just want to confirm some facts. So, let me repeat: the files aren't in his office. So we have to expand the search. For example, they might be in a safe."
[1739] "But, Ellery," the officer objected, who had been listening intently to the exchange between Croining and Ellery, "didn't I tell you this morning that Thomas had checked it out? There are no boxes in Field's safe. It's been confirmed. He has neither a mailbox to be picked up nor a private mailbox – either under his real name or an alias.
[1740] "Thomas also investigated the clubs closely associated with Field and found that the lawyer had no other residence, either fixed or temporary, apart from his apartment on 75th Street. And throughout Thomas's search, there was no sign of any possible hiding place. He thinks Field may have put the files in a package or bag and given it to a shopkeeper to keep. But there's no trace… Weli is an expert in this area, Ellery. I can assure you that your assumption is wrong."
[1741] "I'm doing this for Cronin's sake," Ellery countered, spreading his fingers on the table and blinking. "You see, we must narrow down the search until we can say with certainty: 'It's definitely here.' The office, the safe, and the post office have been ruled out. However, we know that Feld wouldn't keep these files in an inaccessible place. I can't guarantee the file you're looking for, Cronin; it's not the same one we're looking for. We know Feld kept them in a place he could easily reach... Furthermore, we can reasonably infer that he would keep all his important secret files in the same hidden place."
[1742] Cronin scratched his head and nodded.
[1743] "Now we have to apply basic principles, gentlemen," Ellery paused, emphasizing the next sentence. "Since we have narrowed down the investigation to exclude all possible hiding places, leaving only one - the file must be there... there's no difficulty."
[1744] "Let me think," the officer interjected, suddenly becoming somber after his earlier joviality. "Perhaps we should have searched that place more thoroughly, but we didn't."
[1745] "I'm sure our thinking is right," Ellery said firmly. "Just as today is Friday, and tonight there will be three million households eating fish."
[1746] Cronin looked perplexed. "I don't quite understand, Mr. Quin. What do you mean by there being only one possible hiding place?"
[1747] "Field's apartment, Cronin," Ellery said calmly, "The file is there."
[1748] "But I discussed this case with the district attorney yesterday," Cronin objected, "he said you'd already thoroughly searched Field's apartment and found nothing."
[1749] "That's correct - absolutely correct," Ellery said, "we searched Field's apartment and found nothing. The problem is, Cronin, we didn't look in the right place."
[1750] "Well, now that you know, let's go," Cronin exclaimed, springing from his chair.
[1751] The officer gently tapped the red-haired man's knee and pointed to the seat. "Tim, sit down," he advised. "Ellery is just indulging in one of his favorite deduction games. As for where the files are, he knows no more than you do. He's guessing…in detective literature,” he added with a wry smile, "it's called 'deduction.'"
[1752] "I suppose," Ellery muttered, puffing on his cigar, "I'm being questioned again. I'm going back to Field's room to find those elusive files, but I haven't gotten the officer's approval yet."
[1753] "About those files—" the old man began, but was interrupted by the doorbell. Juno opened the door to let Detective Wiley and a small, shifty-looking man enter. The officer stepped in front of them before they could enter the living room. Croning looked at them, and Quinn asked, "Thomas, is that the guy?"
[1754] "You thought you could break in and get away with it, didn't you?" the officer asked the newcomer, kindly but firmly. "You're the one I've been looking for."
[1755] The sneaky-looking young man seemed to freeze. "Ah, you're not pulling my leg, are you?" he stammered.
[1756] The officer smiled reassuringly and led the young man out to the foyer. They whispered back and forth, and the old man nodded in agreement with every word the officer said. Croining and Eliot, sitting in the living room, saw a small note flutter by as the officer slipped it into the young man's tightly clenched hand.
[1757] Quine strode back in. "Okay, Thomas. Take care of other things and make sure our friend doesn't get into trouble...now, gentlemen..."
[1758] Detective Wiley bid a brief farewell and led the apprehensive stranger out of the apartment.
[1759] The officer sat down. "Before we go to Field's room, gentlemen," he said thoughtfully, "I want to clarify a few things. First, based on what Benjamin Morgan told us, Field's business was law, but his main source of income was...extortion. You know that, Tim? Monte Field had drained many influential people, amounting to nearly half a million dollars. In fact, Tim, we believe the motive for murdering Field was related to his recent illicit activities. Without a doubt, the person who killed him was paid a large sum of money to keep quiet and had reached their limit.
[1760] "Tim, you and I both know that extortion mainly relies on the extortionist possessing incriminating documents about someone's unsavory past. That's why we're so sure the files are hidden somewhere...Eliot believes they're in Field's room. Let's see if we can find them. If we do, the archives you've been looking for may resurface, as Eliot pointed out earlier."
[1761] He paused thoughtfully. "Tim, you don't know how much I want to get Field's damn files. They're crucial to me, to unravel many mysteries that have kept us in the dark so far..."
[1762] "Okay, let's get started!" Croining exclaimed, leaping up from his chair. "Do you know how many years I've been tracking Field for this purpose? This will be the happiest day of my life... policeman - let's go!"
[1763] However, Ellery and his father seemed unfazed. They returned to the bedroom to change clothes while Croining paced restlessly in the living room. If Croining had not been so engrossed in his own affairs, he would have noticed the light-hearted mood of the Quin family when he first entered, now shrouded in gloom. Especially the policeman seemed to be in a bad mood, restless, and hesitant to push the investigation forward.
[1764] The Quin family finally dressed and went downstairs. As they got into a taxi, Ellery sighed.
[1765] "Are you afraid of being embarrassed, son?" the old man grumbled into his coat folds.
[1766] "It's not that," Ellery replied, "It's something else… The files will be found, don't worry."
[1767] "I sure hope you're right," Cromwell breathed heavily. The rest of the ride was silent until the taxi pulled up in front of the towering apartment building on 75th Street.
[1768] The trio took the elevator to the fourth floor and stepped into the quiet hallway. The officer glanced around quickly before ringing the doorbell to Field's apartment. There was a rustling sound from behind the door, but no one answered. Suddenly, the door swung open, revealing a red-faced police officer with his hand nervously fidgeting in his back pocket.
[1769] "Hey, don't be scared – we won't bite you!" the officer barked, inexplicably angry, which puzzled Cromwell. He was as jumpy as a racehorse.
[1770] The uniformed officer saluted. "I didn't realize it was you, Officer. I thought someone was casing the place," he said, sounding deflated.
[1771] The three entered the foyer, and the elderly man quickly shut the door with his bony, pale hand.
[1772] "What's going on around here?" Quin asked gruffly, stepping into the living room and looking around.
[1773] "Nothing, sir," the policeman said. "Myself and Cassidy take four-hour shifts, and Ritter checks in occasionally to make sure everything's okay."
[1774] "Oh, he came by, did he?" the old man turned around. "Anyone want to come in here?"
[1775] "No one was here when I was here, sir – and Cassidy wasn't here either," the policeman answered nervously. "We've been on rotation since Tuesday. There hasn't been a soul around these rooms besides Ritter."
[1776]"Stay in the foyer for the next few hours, officer," the officer commanded. "Bring a chair over and take a nap if you want—but if anyone comes to cause trouble, report to us immediately."
[1777]The policeman dragged a chair from the living room and sat with his back against the front door, crossing his arms and closing his eyes.
[1778]The three of them looked grimly at the scene. The porch, though small, was cluttered with various pieces of furniture and decorations. The bookshelves were filled with seemingly unopened books; a modernist table lamp and a few carved ivory ashtrays sat on a small table; two neoclassical chairs; an unusual piece of furniture that looked half like a sideboard and half like a desk; and numerous cushions and small carpets were scattered about. The officer grimaced at the mess.
[1779]"Hey, son—I think the best way to search is for us to go through everything one by one, with one person searching and the other tidying up. I'm not too optimistic about it, I'll say that upfront."
[1780]"The gentleman of the Wailing Wall," Ellery grumbled under his breath, sadness evident on his noble face. "You and me, Cronin—we won't be so pessimistic, will we?"
[1781] Cronin barked, "I mean—less talk, more action, I treat family squabbles with the utmost respect."
[1782] Ellery admiringly gazed at him. "You make decisions like an insect, buddy. More like an ant than a human. Poor Field lies in the morgue... Let's get to work, kids!"
[1783] They began working in front of the dozing policeman, mostly in silence. Ellery's face showed a calm anticipation, the officer displayed grimness and annoyance, and Cronin wore a face of brusqueness and indomitable spirit. The books were taken off the shelves one by one and examined carefully—page by page—the covers were turned over and inspected meticulously, and the backs were pinched and punctured. There were over two hundred books in total, and the search took a long time. After a while, Ellery seemed to let his father and Cronin do the more laborious checks, while he focused on the titles. He once let out a happy exclamation, holding a thin, cheaply bound book up to the light. Cronin immediately jumped forward, his eyes shining. The officer showed a flicker of interest and looked up. But Ellery had only discovered another book on handwriting analysis.
[1784] The old man gazed silently and curiously at his son, pursing his lips thoughtfully. Cronin grumbled and returned to the bookshelf. However, Ellery quickly flipped through the pages and called out again. Their necks stretched over his shoulders to look. The pages of the book were written in pencil with annotations: "Henry Jones," "John Smith," "George Brown." They repeated several times in the blank spaces, as if the person writing was practicing different styles of handwriting.
[1785] "Does Field have a childish habit of scribbling?" Ellery asked, staring intently at the pencil-written words.
[1786] "And what's your brilliant plan, son?" The officer said impatiently, "I know what you mean, but I don't see how this helps us. Except - ah, that's a good idea!"
[1787] He leaned forward, searching again, filled with new interest. Ellery smiled and searched with him. Cronin stared at the two people in confusion.
[1788] "Guys, you should tell me about this too," he said indignantly.
[1789] The officer straightened up. "Ellery suddenly remembered something, and if it's true, it's a stroke of luck for us, reflecting a side of Field's character. This black-hearted scoundrel! Look here, Tim - if someone has a habit of extortion and you keep finding evidence that he is practicing calligraphy according to a textbook, what conclusion will you draw?"
[1790] "You mean he's still a forger?" Cronin frowned, "Although I've been tracking him for years, I never suspected it."
[1791] "He's not just a forger, Cronin," Ellery smiled. "I don't think you'll find Monte Field signing someone else's name on a check or anything like that. He's too cunning to make such a serious mistake. He probably got hold of an original document related to someone and then copied it, sold the copy back to the owner, and kept the original for future use!"
[1792] "If that's the case, Tim," the policeman added ominously, "if we find a hoard of these documents nearby — which I doubt — we may also find the original or originals that led to Monte Field's murder!"
[1793] The red-haired assistant district attorney looked skeptically at his two companions. "There seem to be a lot of 'ifs'," he finally said, shaking his head.
[1794] They continued searching, with fewer and fewer words.
[1795] The porch yielded nothing. After an hour of relentless, exhausting work, they reluctantly concluded that no inch had been left unsearched. The policeman had been thoroughly excited, despite his efforts to suppress it, and it showed in his tight lips and flushed cheeks.
[1796] They resumed their search of the living room, starting with the large closet connected to it. The officer and Ellery once again flipped through the lightweight coats, thick coats, and shawls hanging on the hangers. Nothing was found. There were four hats on the shelf that they had already checked on Tuesday morning: an old Panama hat, a top hat, and two felt hats. Still nothing. Cronin knelt down, looking and tapping the walls and searching for signs of tampering with the woodwork. Nothing was found. The officer stood on a chair and peered into the corners above the shelf. He climbed down and shook his head.
[1797] "Don't bother with the closet, guys," he muttered under his breath. They began to thoroughly search the room and carefully examined the ornate large desk that Hargstrom and Piggott had quickly searched three days ago. There were stacks of papers, canceled bills, and letters they had given to the old man to review. The old Quin had indeed looked at these tattered pieces of paper, as if they contained information written with invisible ink. Finally, he shrugged and threw them away.
[1798] "What a nuisance," he grumbled angrily. "I'm getting old and still not realistic. It's all thanks to the son of a bitch who writes fiction novels."
[1799] He picked up the miscellaneous items he had found in the pockets of the coat from the closet on Tuesday. Ellery frowned, and Cronin wore an indifferent expression. The old man absentmindedly stirred keys, old letters, and wallets, then turned around.
[1800] "There's nothing in the desk," he wearily announced. "I suspect that the cunning prankster would choose such an obvious place to hide something."
[1801] "If he had read Edgar Allan Poe's books, he would have," Ellery grumbled. "Let's continue. Are you sure there's no secret drawer?" he asked Cronin. The redhead shook his head sadly but firmly.
[1802] They searched, rummaged through the furniture, under the carpets and table lamps, behind bookcases and curtain rods. After a series of failures, the desperation of the search was reflected on their faces. After the search, the living room seemed innocent, like a path of a hurricane - a sense of naked, uncomfortable satisfaction arose.
[1803] "Only the bedroom, the small kitchen, and the bathroom left," the police officer told Cronin. The three of them walked into the bedroom where Angela Roxo had slept on Monday night.
[1804] The bedroom was clearly feminine in its furnishings - Ellery attributed this to the influence of the charming Greenwich Village. They searched the room again, and not an inch escaped their watchful eyes and exploring hands; they could only admit defeat once again. They took apart the bedding, checked the springs under the bed; they put it back together and then began to check the wardrobe. Every piece of clothing was firmly fingered, poked, and squeezed - bathrobes, pajamas, shoes, ties. Cronin half-heartedly repeated the check on the walls and the frames of the paintings. They lifted the carpet, moved the chairs, and shook the telephone book on the telephone table by the bedside. The police officer even picked up the metal disc connected to the steam pipe on the floor, just because it was loose, thinking it might contain something.
[1805] They moved from the bedroom to the small kitchen, which was filled with kitchen utensils, making it almost impossible to move around. They quickly searched through a large pantry; Cronin angrily inserted his fingers into the flour and sugar cans. The stove, the cupboard, the cupboard - even the marble laundry tub in the corner - were all systematically checked. There was a box of half-empty bottles of wine standing by the side of the floor. Cronin glanced longingly in that direction, but when the police officer looked at him, he guiltily turned his face away.
[1806] "Now - the bathroom," Ellery murmured. In ominous silence, they all crowded into the tiled bathroom. Three minutes later, they came out, still silent, and walked back into the living room, sitting down in the chairs. The officer took out his snuffbox and pinched a small amount; Cronin and Ellery lit cigarettes.
[1807] "I think, son," the officer was interrupted by the annoying snoring of the policeman in the hallway, and then said gloomily, "I think, let Sherlock Holmes and his followers make a name for themselves. Listen, I'm not blaming you... " But he slumped wearily into the chair.
[1808] Ellery nervously stroked his smooth chin. "I seem to have done a stupid thing," he admitted. "But those documents are somewhere here. Is it a stupid idea? But logic proves my idea. When the total is ten, subtract two, three, and four, there's only one left... Forgive me for being old-fashioned. I insist that the documents are here."
[1809] Cronin snorted and exhaled a cloud of smoke.
[1810] "You can argue," Ellery whispered, leaning back. "Let's check again. No, no! I mean verbally check. Cronin's face fell when Ellery quickly explained, "I mean the apartment of Mr. Field consists of a hallway, a living room, a small kitchen, a bedroom, and a bathroom. We checked the hallway, living room, small kitchen, bedroom, and bathroom, but found nothing. Euclid would have drawn a conclusion here... " He mused, "How did we check these rooms?" He suddenly asked, "We checked the obvious things, and we broke them apart. Furniture, lamps, blankets - I repeat, the obvious things. We tapped the floor, walls, and frames of paintings. It seems nothing escaped the search... "
[1811] He stopped, his eyes brightening. The officer's weary face brightened. He knew from experience that Ellery rarely got excited about trivial matters.
[1812] "However," Ellery said slowly, staring into his father's face. "According to Seneca's Golden Roof, we overlooked something – indeed, we overlooked something!"
[1813] "What!" Croning exclaimed, "You're joking, right?"
[1814] "Oh, I'm not joking," Ellery chuckled, lounging lazily, "we checked the floor and walls, but checked – the ceiling?"
[1815] He spat out the words dramatically, and the other two stared at him in shock.
[1816] "Hey, what do you mean, Ellery?" his father asked, frowning.
[1817] Ellery quickly extinguished his cigarette in the ashtray. "What I mean," he said, "is pure reasoning: in a specific equation, when all possibilities are eliminated except one, then that one possibility, no matter how incredible it may seem, no matter how absurd - it must be correct... According to a similar theorem, I conclude: the document is in this apartment."
[1818] "But, Mr. Quine, heavens - the ceiling!" Croyning exclaimed, the officer glancing guiltily at the ceiling of the living room. Ellery saw this and shook his head with a laugh.
[1819] "I'm not suggesting we get a mason to smash this beautiful ceiling with a sledgehammer," he said, "because I already have the answer. What do these rooms have on their ceilings?"
[1820] "Chandeliers," Croyning answered dubiously, looking up at the full copper fixture above their heads.
[1821] "Oh no—the canopy over the bed!" the officer exclaimed. He jumped up and ran into the bedroom. Cronin followed closely behind, and Ellery strolled behind them, curious.
[1822] They stopped at the foot of the bed and looked up at the canopy. Unlike the standard American canopy, this over-the-top decoration was not just a large piece of fabric hanging from four pillars but was an integral part of the bed. The bed's structure had four pillars extending from the floor to the ceiling, and the thick, dark-brown brocade canopy extended from the ceiling to the floor, connected at the top by a ringed rod, and the brocade folds hung elegantly from this rod.
[1823] "Well, if it's here," the officer muttered, pulling a chair with a silk-covered edge to the side of the bed, "it's gotta be up there. Come on, guys, lend a hand."
[1824] He stood on the chair, stepping on the silk with his shoes without caring about the potential damage. He stretched his arms up, finding that there were still a few feet between him and the ceiling, so he climbed down.
[1825] "Ellery, you can't reach it either," he grumbled. "Field isn't any taller than you. There must be a ladder that Field uses to climb up here."
[1826] Ellery nodded towards the small kitchen, and Corning immediately rushed in. Soon, he emerged with a six-foot-high ladder. The officer climbed to the top rung of the ladder, but even then, his fingers still couldn't reach the rod. Ellery motioned for his father to come down and climbed up himself to solve the problem. He stood on the ladder to get a better view of the top of the canopy.
[1827] He grabbed the velvet and pulled it down. The entire canopy gave way, leaning to one side, revealing a twelve-inch-deep wooden panel — a framework hidden behind the drapery. Ellery's fingers quickly scanned the wood carvings on the panel. Corning and the officer stared up at him with uncertain expressions. Ellery couldn't find an entrance, so he leaned forward and felt around the bottom of the panel for the velvet.
[1828] "Pull it down!" the officer exclaimed.
[1829] Ellery yanked the fabric, and the entire canopy's velvet fell onto the bed, revealing the unadorned, unadorned bottom of the panel.
[1830] "It's hollow," Ellery tapped the bottom of the panel with his knuckles.
[1831] "That's not helpful," Croning said. "Anyway, it won't be a solid block of wood. Why don't you try the other side of the bed, Mr. Quine?"
[1832] But Erikr, who had moved aside, checked the side of the panel again and called out triumphantly. He had been looking for a complex, undisclosed "secret door" - now he found that the secret door was just a sliding panel that was hard to notice. It was cleverly hidden - the joint between the sliding panel and the fixed panel was covered by a row of wood-carved rose motifs and rough decorations - but that didn't matter, any beginner in a detective story would be thrilled by this exemplary hiding technique.
[1833] "It seems to be proving me right, doesn't it?" Erikr chuckled, peering into the darkness of the hole he had discovered. He stretched his long arms into the hole. The officer and Croning held their breath and stared at him intently.
[1834] "By the gods," Erikr suddenly shouted, his agile body trembling with excitement. "Remember what I said, dad? Those documents will be - in the hat!"
[1835] His sleeve was covered in dust. He withdrew his arm and the two people below him saw a silk top hat with a musty smell in his hand!
[1836] As Ellery threw the hat onto the bed and reached back into the open-mouthed hole, Cronin danced a complex jig. Soon, Ellery pulled out another hat - another - and another! They lay on the bed - two silk hats, two top hats.
[1837] "Take this flashlight, son," the policeman instructed. "Look inside to see if there's anything else."
[1838] Ellery took the flashlight handed to him and shone it into the hole. After a moment, he climbed down and shook his head.
[1839] "That's it," he said, shaking off his sleeve. "But I think that's enough."
[1840] The policeman picked up the four hats and took them to the living room, placing them on the sofa. The three sat down solemnly, gazing at each other.
[1841] "I'm eager to know the truth," Cronin finally whispered.
[1842] "I'm afraid to see the truth," the officer countered.
[1843] "Mene, mene, tekel, upharsin," Ellery laughed. "In this case, it can be interpreted as 'inscriptions on the board.' Keep checking, Macduff!"
[1844] The officer picked up one of the silk hats, with a simple Brown Brothers label on the luxurious silk. He peeled off the lining, finding nothing inside, and tried to pull off the leather sweatband, but could not with all his strength. He borrowed a knife from Cronin and laboriously cut off the band. Then he looked up.
[1845] "This hat, Romans and countrymen," he said with a smile, "has nothing inside but the material of a hat you're familiar with. Would you like to check it?"
[1846] Corning yelled and snatched the hat from the officer's hand. Enraged, he almost tore the hat into pieces.
[1847] "Damn it!" he said, throwing the remains of the hat on the ground. "Can you explain this to me, officer?"
[1848] Quinn smiled and picked up the second silk hat, curiously examining it.
[1849] "You're in a tough spot, Tim," he said. "We know why one of these hats is empty. Right, Ellery?"
[1850] "Miles." Ellery muttered.
[1851] "That's right – Mickles," the officer replied.
[1852] "Charles Mickles," Cronin exclaimed. "Field's right-hand man! How did he get involved in this?"
[1853] "Not yet clear. Do you know him?"
[1854] "Only that he's always trailing behind Field. He was a long-time convict, you know?"
[1855] "Yes, I know," the officer answered distractedly. "We'll discuss Mickles' period later... I'll explain the hat situation first: According to Mickles, he prepared Field's evening wear for the night of the murder, including a silk hat. Mickles swears that, to his knowledge, Field only had one top hat. Now, let's assume that Field hid the documents in the hat and was wearing the hat with the documents when he was heading to the Roman Theater. He must have replaced the empty hat with the one containing the documents. Since he was so careful to only put a silk hat in the closet, he would have realized that Mickles would be suspicious when he found a top hat. So, when he replaced the hats, he had to hide the empty hat. He should have put it in the place where he got the hat with the documents – the shelf above the bed. Isn't that natural?"
[1856] "Oh, that's certain!” Crowning called out.
[1857] "Finally,” the officer continued, "we can be absolutely sure that Field was very careful with the hats. He planned to put the hat he wore at the Roman Theater back in the hiding place when he went home. Then, he would have taken the hat you just tore apart and put it back in the closet... Let's keep going."
[1858] He ripped off the inner band of the second silk hat and saw that it also had the Brown Brothers label. "Look at this!” he called out. Both men bent down and saw that the surface of the inner layer of the belt was written in purple ink with a clear print: Benjamin Morgan.
[1859] "I want you to swear to keep this confidential, Tim,” the officer immediately turned to the red-haired man and said, "Don't reveal in any way that you witnessed Benjamin's involvement in this case."
[1860] "What do you take me for, officer?” Crowning shouted angrily. "I'll keep it to myself, believe me!"
[1861] "Okay," Quin stroked the lining of the hat, which made a clear crackling sound.
[1862] "Now," Ellery said calmly, "we have our first clear indication of why the killer had to take Field's hat on Monday night. The killer's name is likely to be written in the same way - it's an indelible ink, you know - the killer wouldn't leave a hat with his name on it at the crime scene."
[1863] "If you find that hat," Corning called out, "you'll know who the killer is!"
[1864] "Tim, I'm afraid," the officer replied calmly, "that the hat is gone forever."
[1865] He pointed to the bottom of the inner band connected to the lining. He quickly pulled off those stitches, stuck his fingers into the lining and the hat top, and then silently pulled out a bundle of documents tied together with a thin rubber band.
[1866] "If I were as disliked as some people think," Ellery said, leaning back and thinking aloud, "I would say with complete fairness: 'I told you so.'"
[1867] "We know when to expect a hit from you, son - don't repeat yourself," the officer chuckled, breaking the rubber band and quickly scanning the papers, smiling in satisfaction, and putting them in his jacket pocket.
[1868] "Morgan's, right," he said briefly, and began to examine another round-top hat. The inside of the sweatband had a vague X marked on it. The officer found a row of stitches similar to those in the silk hat lining. He pulled out the file - thicker than Morgan's bundle - and curiously checked it. He then handed it to Cronin, who's fingers were trembling.
[1869] "Lucky for you, Tim," he said slowly, "the person you're tracking is dead, but this involves a lot of high-profile people. I think you'll find yourself a hero in a few days."
[1870] Cronin grabbed the bundle of papers, excitedly spreading them out. "They're here - they're here!" he exclaimed, stuffing the stack of papers into his pocket.
[1871] "I have to go, officer," he said urgently. "There's a lot of work to do – and what you found in the fourth hat is none of my business. I'm grateful to you and Mr. Quinn! Goodbye!"
[1872] He rushed out of the room, and a moment later, the snoring of the policeman in the hallway stopped. The outside door slammed shut.
[1873] Ellery and the officer exchanged glances.
[1874] "I don't see how these things are of any use to us," the old man muttered, running his fingers over the sweatband of the last top hat. "We've discovered things and made deductions at a much faster pace than we anticipated – oh well..." He sighed, holding the band up to the light.

[1876] Chapter 18: The Standoff
[1877] On Friday afternoon, while Inspector Quin, Ellery, and Tim Cronin were searching the Montague Field house, Officer Wiley, as usual, walked slowly up the brownstone steps of the Quin residence from Broadway to Eighty-seventh Street, rang the bell. Juno answered with a cheerful voice, and the officer came up sternly.
[1878] "The officer's not home!" Juno announced impulsively, her thin body completely hidden behind the large apron. The smell of onion-fried steak filled the air.
[1879] "Get out of here, kid!" Wiley shouted. He pulled out a sealed envelope from his chest pocket and handed it to Juno, "Give it to the officer when he gets back. If you forget, I'll dump you in the East River."
[1880] "And who else?" Juno whispered, his lips trembling, and then he politely added, "Yes, sir."
[1881] "Well, then." Wiley turned calmly and went downstairs to the street, his broad back very visible, in stark contrast to Juno's grin in the window on the fourth floor.
[1882] Around six o'clock, the two Quinns returned to their residence, and Officer Wiley suddenly noticed the official envelope lying on his plate.

[1911] Report on Francis Evans-Pope
[1912] (September 28, 19XX)
[1913] After being released from the manager's office at the Roman Theater on Monday evening by Inspector Quin, Francis Evans-Pope immediately left the theater. [1930] The signature was the familiar and careless initials of a name that Willy knew.

I believe you are just going to have to rely on luck in this matter. You will never trace the poison that killed Monte Field. This is not only my humble opinion, but also that of the chief and Jones. [1937] Ellery smiled. "Of course, do you remember Perianthus? … Ah? You may be polite, sir … Perianthus of Collins Bay, who said in his lucid moments, 'Diligence can do anything!'"
[1938] The fire was roaring, and Jones curled up in his favorite corner on the floor. Ellery smoked, comfortably gazing at the flames, while old Quin filled his nose with snuff. The two Quins settled down to a serious discussion. More precisely – Quin Inspector settled down, talking seriously to his son, as Ellery seemed to be in an extremely dreamy mood, far from the unpleasant details of crime and punishment.
[1939] The old man slapped his hand on the armrest of the chair. "Ellery, have you ever seen such a perplexing case in your life?"
[1940] "On the contrary," Ellery commented, half-closing his eyes and staring at the fire, "it's quite natural for you to be nervous. You're getting worked up over something as trivial as arresting the killer. Please forgive my hedonistic philosophy … if you recall, in my story called 'The Black Window Incident,' the detective I wrote about had no difficulty capturing the criminals. Why? Because they stayed calm. Conclusion: always stay calm … I'm thinking about tomorrow. A pleasant holiday!"
[1941] "For a gentleman, son," the officer barked angrily, "your lack of coherence is surprising. Your words mean nothing, and your silence speaks volumes. No, I'm confused... "
[1942] Ellery burst into laughter. "Maine's forests - brown - lakeside charming cabins - fishing rods - air - oh, Lord, will tomorrow ever come?"
[1943] The elder Quine looked at his son with a mixture of pity and warmth. "I... I hope... Hmm, don't mind. I mean, Ellery, if my little thief fails... Then it's all over."
[1944] "Damn thief!" Ellery exclaimed. "What does Pon have to do with human suffering? My next book is almost finished, dad."
[1945] "Stealing inspiration from real life, you scoundrel," the old man muttered, "if you use the case of Field as a plot, I would be very interested in reading the last few chapters you wrote!"
[1946] "Poor Dad!" Ellery chuckled, "Don't take life so seriously. Fail and fail, as they say. Anyway, Monte Field wasn't worth a dime."
[1947] "That's not the point," the old man said, "I don't want to admit defeat… The motives and conspiracies in this case are tangled like a ball of yarn, Ellery. In my entire career, I've never encountered such a tough nut to crack. It's enough to give you a stroke! I know who killed the person—I know why they did it, and I even know how they did it! But where am I?” He paused, took out a pinch of snuff, "I'm in the middle of nowhere, right there!” He roared, then gradually calmed down.
[1948] "Certainly a very unusual situation," Ellery murmured, "However, the more difficult problem has been solved… Hey! I can't wait to bathe in the idyllic stream!"
[1949] "You might get pneumonia," the officer said worriedly, "Now promise me, young man, that you won't do any of those silly things that go against nature while you're there. I don't want to plan a funeral—I…"
[1950] Ellery suddenly fell silent, looking at his father. The officer's face seemed unusually aged under the flickering light of the fire, and the pain on his face made his deep wrinkles more human. His hands looked fragile and worried, gently brushing back his thick gray hair.
[1951] Ellery stood up, blushing and hesitated for a moment before quickly bending forward and patting his father's shoulder.
[1952] "Cheer up, dad," he whispered. "If it weren't for Sandford and me arranging things... everything would be fine—believe me. If there's any way I can help you, even a little... but there isn't. It's your job now, dad, and no one can handle this problem better than you in the world..."
[1953] He disappeared into the bedroom. Juno sat in the corner in a Turkish manner, quickly getting up and walking across the room to the officer's chair. He sat on the floor, leaning his head against the old man's knees. The silence was occasionally broken by the crackling of wood in the fireplace and the dull sound of Ellery moving around in the next room.
[1954] Inspector Quin was tired. His face was pale, gaunt, and wrinkled, looking like a relief in the dim red light. He stroked Juno's curly hair.
[1955] "Juno, boy," he whispered, "don't become a policeman when you grow up."
[1956] Junna turned his head, looking solemnly at the old man. "I want to be like you," he announced.
[1957] The phone rang, and the old man jumped up. He grabbed the phone from the table, his face pale and his voice choked. "This is Quin," he said.
[1958] After a moment, he put down the phone and staggered towards the bedroom. He leaned heavily against the doorframe. Ellery straightened up from his suitcase - and jumped forward.
[1959] "Dad!" he cried, "What's wrong?"
[1960] The inspector tried to smile. "It's just... a little tired, son, I think," he grunted, "I just got a tip from a small-time crook... "
[1961] "That-"
[1962] "He found nothing."
[1963] Ellery took his father's arm and led him to a chair by the bedside. The old man slumped into the chair, his eyes tired and weary. "Ellery, son," he said. "The last piece of evidence is gone. It's enough to drive you crazy! There's no solid evidence in court to prove the killer guilty. What do we have? A series of perfect reasoning - that's all. A good lawyer can find loopholes in the case... Oh! It's not over yet."
[1964] He got up from the chair, suddenly serious. He patted Ellery's broad back with vigor. "Go to bed, son," he said. "You have to get up early tomorrow. I'll stay up and think about it."
[1965] Interlude
[1966] Please note
[1967] The current detective novel craze places the reader in the position of the chief detective. I convinced Ellery Queen to allow me to add a challenge to the reader in "The Roman Hat Mystery" – "Who killed Monte Field?" "How did the murderer commit the crime?" Mr. Queen agreed with my point of view, and the quick-witted detective novel researcher should now be able to draw a clear conclusion to these questions at this stage of the story. The answer – or evidence that accurately points to the criminal – can be derived through a series of logical reasoning and psychological observations... Let me conclude with a warning to the reader: "Reader, beware!"
[1968] J.J.McC.
