[1970] Part Four
[1971] "The perfect criminal is like a superhero. He must be meticulous in his methods: he is a lone wolf who cannot be detected and is invisible. He has no friends and is alone. He must be careful not to make mistakes, must have a sharp mind and quick reflexes ... but that doesn't matter, such a person still exists ... On the other hand, he must be favored by fate - even if he faces uncontrollable situations, he will not be ruined. I think this is even more difficult ... But the last point is the hardest. He must never repeat his crime, the weapon he used, or the motive for his crime! ... In my forty years of police career in the United States, I have not encountered or investigated such a perfect criminal.
[1972] - Excerpt from Richard Queen: "Crime and Investigation Methods in America"
[1973] Chapter 19: Queen's Further Interrogation
[1974] On Saturday night, Richard Queen's behavior was unexpected and surprising, especially for the district attorney, Sampson. The old man became irritable and angry, completely unreasonable. He bit his lips, muttered to himself, and paced nervously around Louis Pincer's office. He didn't even notice the presence of Sampson, Pincer, and another person. That person had never appeared in the theater office before. He sat in Pincer's large chair like a mouse, with eyes as big as teacups. This bright-eyed person was Zuna, who accompanied his melancholy master to the Roman Theater to investigate the case, a rare honor.
[1975] In fact, Queen's mood was very low. Throughout his career, he had encountered countless clearly unsolvable problems and turned them around. Therefore, the strange behavior of the police officer was even more puzzling to Sampson. He had dealt with the old man for many years and had never seen him so agitated.
[1976] The old man's mood was not due to the progress of the investigation of the Field case, as Sampson feared. Juana sat with his mouth open in the corner, the only one who knew why the officer was pacing nervously. With his quick wits and innate insight, as well as his long-standing friendly relationship with the old man, Juana was very familiar with his temperament. He knew that his master's actions were only because Ellery was not present. That morning, Ellery was sent off to the station by his father in a melancholy mood, taking the 7:45 express train to leave New York. At the last moment, the young man changed his mind and announced that he would abandon his decision to travel to Maine and instead stay in New York to accompany his father until the case was closed. The old man disagreed. With his understanding of his son's character, he sensed how much the easily excited son longed for this holiday that had been coming for over a year. He didn't want to deprive his son of a pleasant journey just because of himself, so he was annoyed that his son wanted to stay by his side.
[1977] He ignored Ellery's proposal, pushed him onto the train, and reluctantly smiled and patted him goodbye. As the train pulled out of the station, Ellery's last words came from the platform: "I'll miss you, Dad. I'll write to you as soon as I can!"
[1978] The officer was now feeling the pain of separation from his son. He paced back and forth, fraying Pensler's carpet. He felt dizzy, weak, and nauseous, out of sync with the world and people, but he didn't hide his anger.
[1979] "It's about time, Pensler," he shouted at the short, stout manager, "how long will it take to get rid of these damn audience?"
[1980] "Soon, officer, very soon," Pensler replied. The district attorney blew his nose, which was running due to a cold. Juana stared in awe at his god in his mind.
[1981] There was a knock on the door, and everyone turned their heads. The rumpled promoter Harry Nelson stuck his rough face into the room. "Officer, mind if I join this little gathering?" he asked cheerfully, "I just started, is it about to end - oh, if you allow it, I'll stay here for a while!"
[1982] The officer gave him a cold glance from under his thick eyebrows. His posture was like Napoleon, his hair standing on end, his muscles bulging, and his face hostile. Sampson was surprised to see him. Quin had never shown his unexpected temperament.
[1983] "Well, it doesn't matter," he barked, "there are enough people here anyway."
[1984] Nelson's face turned red, and he seemed to want to leave. The officer blinked his eyes and felt better.
[1985] "Here - sit down, Nelson," he said politely, "don't take it personally with an old man like me. I'm just a little tired. Tonight, I may need your help."
[1986] "I'd be delighted to join in, officer," Nelson grinned, "What's this about – the Spanish Inquisition?"
[1987] "Pretty much," the officer lowered his eyebrows, "but - it depends on the situation."
[1988] At this moment, the door opened and the towering and robust Sheriff Wiley strode into the room. He handed a piece of paper to the officer.
[1989] "Everyone's here, sir," he said.
[1990] "Has everyone gone out?" he said loudly.
[1991] "Yes, sir. I sent the janitor down to the lounge, and they can't come back until we're done. The cashier has gone home, and so have the male and female ushers. The actors are backstage, I think they're changing clothes."
[1992] "Alright. Let's go, gentlemen," the officer strode out of the room, followed closely by Juno, who had been silent all evening, just quietly making approving noises. This scene was observed by the district attorney, who for some unknown reason was amused.
[1993] The auditorium was once again empty, with rows of empty seats cold and vacant. The lights in the theater were all turned on, casting a dim glow over the front rows of the stalls.
[1994] As the five men and Juno quickly made their way to the left aisle, someone's head bobbed in the left seating area. It was clear that a group of people were waiting for the officer's arrival. The officer slowly walked down the aisle, choosing a seat in front of the left box, so that everyone sitting could face him. Poulson, Nelson, and Sampson stood at one end of the aisle, while Juno watched excitedly by their side.
[1995] This group of people was arranged in a peculiar way. The officer stood in the middle of the front row, and all the seats occupied from there to the back were directly next to the aisle. The last two rows of the twelfth row were filled with all kinds of people – men and women, young and old. These people had been sitting in the seats they were now occupying when the crime occurred, and the officer had personally checked them after finding the body. In the area of eight seats – Monty Field's seat and the surrounding empty seats – sat William Pusak, Esther Jablow, Maggie O'Connell, Jesse Lynch, and Johnny the Parson – "the priest" with a sneaky look, looking uneasy and covering his mouth with yellow-stained fingers, whispering to the usheress.
[1996] The police officer suddenly made a gesture, and everyone fell silent. Sampson looked around at the chandeliers and lights, the empty theater, and the lowered curtain, feeling that the stage was set for the revelation of an exciting truth. He leaned forward with interest. Poulson and Nelson quietly observed the scene. Juno stared intently at the elderly man.
[1997] "Ladies and gentlemen," Quin looked at the group, getting straight to the point, "I have brought you here for a specific purpose. I will not keep you here for longer than necessary. But what is necessary and what is not, is entirely up to me. If my questions do not receive the truthful answers I believe they should, then everyone will have to stay here until I am satisfied. I hope that before I continue, you all understand my meaning."
[1998] He paused, looking around. There was a stir among the crowd, and suddenly there was a murmur, but it quickly subsided.

[2001] As the officer began to delve into the matter, several people entered the theater through the back door. Sampson greeted them quietly. [2003] "Will the officer object to us staying here to listen?" Barry asked nervously, glancing at the officer, who stopped and stared coldly in their direction.
[2004] "I don't know..." Sampson said worriedly, as Eve Ellis quietly shushed them, and they fell silent.
[2005] "Now -" the officer said sternly after the commotion subsided, "this is the situation. Remember, you are back to Monday evening. The second act's curtain has risen, and the lights have dimmed. There is commotion on stage, and you are all focused on the thrilling scene of the play... Did any of you, especially those sitting in the aisle seats, notice anything strange, unusual, or unsettling happening around or near you at that time?"
[2006] He paused expectantly. Everyone shook their heads blankly and fearfully. No one answered.
[2007] "Think hard," the officer said loudly, "You remember that I asked you the same way along this aisle on Monday night. Of course, I don't expect you to lie, and if you can't remember what happened on Monday night, I don't have any reason to expect you to tell me anything amazing. But the situation is very urgent. Someone was murdered here, and frankly, we have to get to the bottom of it. It's one of our most challenging cases! We are blank and clueless - I am being open and honest with you, and I hope you will be too - given the situation, I must ask for your help. Only a few of you saw something important five days ago, and if something important comes up... based on my experience, people often forget some details under the pressure of tension and excitement, and after a few hours, days, or weeks, when they calm down, they gradually remember. I hope that happens to you..."
[2008] The officer's tone was sharp, and the people around him listened intently, momentarily forgetting their tension. After he finished speaking, people began to whisper, murmur, or occasionally shake their heads and quietly argue. The officer patiently waited.
[2009] "If there's anything to say, please raise your hand..."
[2010] A woman hesitated before raising her pale hand.
[2011] "Oh, Mrs.," Quin's finger pointed to her, "Do you remember anything unusual?"

[2013] "Really? That's interesting, ma'am," the officer said. "What time was that – do you remember?"
[2014] "I don't remember the time, sir," the old lady said sharply. "But it was probably around ten minutes after the curtain rose."
[2015] "I see… Do you remember what she looked like – young or old? What clothes was she wearing?"
[2016] The old lady looked puzzled. "I'm not sure, sir," she quivered. "I didn't notice –"
[2017] A clear and loud voice came from the back row. Everyone turned around. Maggie O'Connell stood up.
[2018] "You don't have to bother with that, officer," she said coldly. "The lady saw me walking up and down the aisle. You know what I'm talking about." She gave the officer a disdainful look.
[2019] People gasped. The old lady looked uncertainly at the usher, then at the officer, and finally sat down slowly.
[2020] "That's not surprising," the officer said calmly. "Well, are there any other witnesses?"
[2021] No one answered. Quinn realized that perhaps people didn't want to reveal their thoughts in front of others. So he walked down the aisle to each row, asking each person in a voice only they could hear. After asking everyone, he slowly returned to his seat.
[2022] "It seems I must let everyone return to their homes by the fireside," he said. "Thank you for your help... adjourned!"
[2023] He said to them. The crowd stared at him, confused, and then whispered among themselves as they stood up, picked up their clothes and hats, and filed out of the theater under Wiley's stern gaze. Hilda Orland stood at the back of the crowd, sighing.

[2025] The actors mingled with the crowd as they left the theater.

[2027] He sat in a seat, crossing his hands behind his back and looking at Maggie O'Connell, Parson Johnny, and the others.

If you know - come clean!"

No, sir, I don't know...he was pretty decent to me—helped me clear a few charges," he admitted brazenly, "but I really—just didn't know he was here Monday night."

[2059]Chapter XX: Michaelis wrote a letter
[2060]For the first time in a week, the Quinns walked into his small office in the headquarters building, throwing his coat over the chair.

[2080] The note had no date or salutation. It began abruptly.
[2081] The writer was Charles Michaels, I think you know me. For over two years, I've been Monte Field's right-hand man.
[2082] I'll come straight to the point. Last Monday night, you killed Monte Field at the Romeo Theater. Monte Field told me on Sunday night that he was going to meet you at the Romeo Theater, and I was the only one who knew.
[2083] There's one more thing. I know why you killed him. You killed Field to get the papers in his hat. But you didn't know that the papers in his hat weren't the originals. To prove this to you, I've attached a copy of Monte Field's testimony of Inner Johnson in the letter. If the papers you got from Monte Field's hat still exist, compare them with this one. You'll soon see that I've given you the real thing. The rest of the originals are still in my hands, in a place you'll never find. I dare say the police are looking for these papers. What do you think would happen if I walked into Officer Quinn's office with these papers and told him this story?
[2084] I'll give you a chance to buy back these papers. You can bring twenty-five thousand dollars in cash to the place I specify, and then I'll give you the papers. I need money, and you need the papers and my silence.
[2085] Meet me tomorrow, Tuesday at midnight, at the seventh bench on the sidewalk at the corner of 59th Street and Fifth Avenue in Central Park. I'll be wearing a gray overcoat and a gray wide-brimmed hat. Just say "papers" to me.
[2086] This is the only way you can get these documents. Don't try to find me before the agreed time. If you don't show up, I know what I should do.
[2087] The letter, with its messy handwriting and crowded letters, was signed "Charles Michaels."
[2088] The inspector sighed, licked the glue layer of the envelope, and sealed it. He calmly gazed at the name and address written in the same handwriting on the envelope, nonchalantly affixing a stamp in the corner.
[2089] He pressed another button. The door opened, and Detective Ritter walked in.
[2090] "Good morning, Inspector."
[2091] "Good morning, Reiter." The officer thoughtfully weighed the letter in his hand. "What have you been up to?"
[2092] Detective Reiter's feet shuffled. "Nothing special, officer. I'm helping Officer Quin until Saturday, but I haven't received any work on the Field case this morning."
[2093] "Oh, I have a nice little task for you." The officer suddenly grinned and handed the letter over. Reiter took it with a puzzled look. "Here you go, kid. Go to the corner of 149th Street and Third Avenue and drop this letter into the nearest mailbox!"
[2094] Reiter stared at the letter, scratched his head, looked at Quin, and finally stuffed the letter into his coat pocket.
[2095] The officer leaned back in his swivel chair, satisfied, and took a pinch of snuff.
[2096] Chapter XXI: Capture
[2097] On October 2nd, Tuesday night at 11:30 PM, a tall man with a soft black hat and a black coat emerged from the lobby of a small hotel near 53rd Street on 7th Avenue. He strode quickly down 7th Avenue towards Central Park.
[2098] At 59th Street, he turned east and walked along the deserted street towards 5th Avenue. When he reached the outer edge of the circular square at the entrance to Central Park, he stopped in the shadow of a large concrete pillar, leaning lazily against it. He lit a cigarette, and the light from the match illuminated his face. It was an old face with a few wrinkles, a tuft of gray-white hair sticking out from under his hat, and a small, white beard hanging from his upper lip. The match flickered out.
[2099] He stood quietly against the concrete pillar, his hands in his coat pockets, smoking. If one observed closely, they would notice that his fingers were trembling slightly, and his black shoes tapped nervously on the ground.
[2100] The cigarette went out, and he dropped it, glancing at his wristwatch. The hands pointed to 11:50 PM. He grumbled impatiently and walked through the park's main entrance. As he walked up the path, the arc lights around the circular square dimmed. He hesitated, not yet deciding where to go. He looked around, thought for a moment, and then sat heavily on the first bench - as if a tired man wanted to rest quietly in the darkness of the park.
[2101] His head slowly drooped; his body relaxed. He seemed to be dozing off.
[2102] Time passed slowly. No one passed by the black-clad man sitting on the bench. Cars roared past on 5th Avenue. The sharp whistle of traffic police occasionally cut through the cold air. The cold wind blew through the tree branches. A girl's clear laughter came from a dark corner of the park – gentle and distant, yet crystal clear. Time passed in a daze; the man slept even deeper.
[2103] However, when the nearby church chimed the hour of midnight, the man's body tensed. He waited for a moment, then stood up abruptly.
[2104] Instead of heading towards the entrance, he turned around and continued walking slowly and heavily down the path, his sharp and alert gaze hidden in the shadows cast by his hat and collar. He walked steadily and unhurriedly, as if counting the benches. Two – three – four – five – he stopped. In the dim darkness ahead, he could vaguely see a gray figure sitting quietly on a bench.
[2105] He approached slowly. Six – seven – he didn't stop, walking straight ahead. Eight – nine – ten... Then he turned around and walked back, his steps lighter and more decisive. He approached the seventh bench quickly and stopped suddenly. Suddenly, he seemed to have made up his mind and sat down in the place where the vague figure was resting quietly. The figure grumbled and made room for the newcomer.
[2106] The two sit in silence. After a while, the man in black reaches into his coat pocket and pulls out a pack of cigarettes. He lights one and holds the burning match for a moment. By the light, he sneaks a peek at the silent man sitting beside him. But the match burns out too quickly, and he can't see anything - the man sitting on the bench is wrapped up just as tightly as he is. The match goes out, and the two are once again surrounded by darkness.
[2107] The man in black seems to have made up his mind. He leans forward and suddenly taps the man's knee, saying in a low, hoarse voice: "File!"
[2108] The other man immediately wakes up. He turns half his body and examines the man beside him, muttering something that seems satisfied. He cautiously turns his body, his right hand wearing gloves reaching into the right pocket of his coat. The first man eagerly leans forward, his eyes shining. The man sitting beside him extends his hand from his pocket, tightly holding something.
[2109] The owner of the hand does something remarkable. He suddenly leaps up from the bench and jumps back from the first man. At the same time, he extends his right arm towards the hunched-over, wide-eyed man in black. The arc lamp shines dimly in the distance, casting a faint light on the thing in his hand - a revolver.
[2110] The first man shrieks hoarsely and jumps up like a cat. His hand darts into his coat pocket. He lunges at the man in front of him, tense and oblivious to the revolver pointing at his heart.
[2111] But an incredible thing happened: the open space, the quiet scene of a countryside suddenly became tense and chaotic – rolling, roaring, and great confusion. A group of men with guns suddenly emerged from the bushes a few feet behind the bench. At the same time, another group of men with guns appeared from the other end of the path and ran towards the two men. From both ends of the path – about 100 feet from the entrance to the park – several armed police officers ran towards them. The four groups were about to converge.
[2112] The man who drew the gun and jumped up from the bench fired before the reinforcements arrived. A loud bang echoed through the park, and a cluster of orange flames struck the man in black, causing him to lean forward and clutch his shoulder in pain. His knees buckled, and he collapsed to the ground, still fumbling in his pocket.
[2113] But the oncoming people subdued him, and he was no longer able to do any harm. He was pinned to the ground, his hands held tightly. They remained silent until a crisp voice rang out behind them: "Be careful, kids – watch his hand!"
[2114] Detective Richard Quinn pushed his way through the panting crowd and stood thoughtfully in front of the man rolling on the ground. "Take his hand out, Wiley – slowly, grip it tight, or he'll stab you."
[2115] The man on the ground struggled desperately, and Detective Thomas Wiley held his arm tightly, carefully pulling his hand out of his pocket. The hand emerged – nothing was there, and the muscles relaxed at the last moment. The two men quickly cuffed him.
[2116] Willie was about to search his pockets when the officer sternly stopped him, bending down to the man rolling on the ground.
[2117] The old man cautiously reached into the man's pocket, as if his life depended on it. He grabbed something, carefully pulled it out, and held it up to the light.
[2118] It was a hypodermic syringe. The white liquid inside glimmered under the arc light.
[2119] The officer smiled and knelt in front of the injured man, snatching off his black woolen hat.
[2120] "It's all a disguise," he muttered.
[2121] He ripped off the person's gray beard and wiped over the wrinkled face with his hand. The person's skin immediately smeared.
[2122] "Well, well," the officer whispered, and the person glared at him. "Glad to see you again, Mr. Stephen Barry, and your dear friend, tetraethyl lead."
